The Obligation to Disclose Defects and Defloration in Murabahah
Commentary on the requirement to disclose defects and defloration in Murabahah sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that disclosure of defects and defloration in Murabahah is wajib and identify which defects must be disclosed.

Core Idea
In Murabahah, the seller must disclose any defect that reduces the commodity's value, even if caused by the buyer or a third party.

Murabahah is a trust-based sale where the seller must disclose the cost price and any changes affecting value. This lesson focuses on the requirement to disclose defects—including defloration—that occur after the seller's purchase. The text explains why disclosure is necessary and gives examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling on profit (Murabahah). The ruling from the matn is that the seller must disclose (yubayyin) any defect or defloration. This is a wajib obligation.
The source uses 'وببيان' which indicates obligation.
2
Understand the reason for disclosure
Defects diminish the commodity's value, so the buyer deserves a price reduction. Failure to disclose deceives the buyer, which is prohibited. The text says 'لأنها صارت مقصودة بالإتلاف فيقابلها شيء من الثمن' (because it became intended for destruction, so something of the price corresponds to it).
3
Identify the types of defects that require disclosure
Any defect that reduces value must be disclosed, whether caused by the buyer or someone else. Examples: tearing of a garment by folding, mouse gnawing, fire burning. Also defloration of a virgin slave-girl because the hymen is part of the commodity.
The text says 'وشمل ما إذا تكسر الثوب بنشره وطيه' and 'دخل تحت الأول ما إذا أصاب الثوب قرض فأر أو حرق نار'.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
If a buyer finds a defect after purchase and accepts it, the seller may still sell on profit at the original price, but must disclose the defect if reselling on profit (as explained later in the commentary).
This step touches on the next lesson; focus on the core obligation here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب المرابحة والتولية قوله : (وببيان بالتعيب ووطء البكر) أي يرابح مع البيان إذا عيبه المشتري أو غيره لأنها صارت مقصودة بالإتلاف فيقابلها شيء من الثمن، وكذا إذا وطئها وهي بكر لأن العذرة جزء من العين فيقابلها شيء من الثمن وقد حبسها. وشمل ما إذا تكسر الثوب بنشره وطيه، ودخل تحت الأول ما إذا أصاب الثوب قرض فأر أو حرق نار والقرض بالقاف والفاء والتعيب مصدر عيبه إذا أحدث فيه عيباً. وأطلقنا في تعييب غير المشتري فشمل ما إذا أخذ المشتري الأرش أو لا، وما إذا كان بأمر المشتري أو بغير أمره، وما وقع في الهداية من التقييد بقوله وأخذ المشتري أرشه اتفاقي للوجوب كما في فتح القدير. ثم اعلم أن زفر قال : لا يرابح إلا بالبيان في المسألتين واختاره الفقيه أبو الليث فقال : وقول زفر أجود وبه نأخذ. ورجحه في فتح القدير. وأشار المؤلف رحمه الله تعالى بالمسألة الأولى إلى أنه إذا وجد بالمبيع عيباً فرضي به كان له أن يبيعه مرابحة على الثمن الذي اشتراه به لأن الثابت له خيار، فإسقاطه لا يمنع من البيع مرابحة كما لو كان فيه خيار شرط أو رؤية، وكذا لو اشتراه مرابحة فاطلع على خيانة فرضي به كان له أن يبيعه مرابحة على ما أخذه به لما ذكرنا أن الثابت له مجرد خيار؛ كذا في فتح القدير قوله : ( ورجحه في فتح القدير) قال في الفتح : لأن حسن مبنى المرابحة على عدم الخيانة وعدم ذكره أنها انتقصت إيهام للمشتري أن الثمن المذكور كان لها ناقصة والغالب أنه لو علم أن ذلك ثمنها صحيحة لم يأخذها معيبة إلا بحطيطة ثم قال : لكن قولهم هو كما لو تغير السعر بأمر الله تعالى فإنه لا يجب أن يبين أنه اشتراه في حال غلائه، وكذا لو اصفر الثوب لطول مكثه أو توسخ الزام قوي .اهـ. قال في النهر : وقد يفرق بأن الإيهام مع تغير السعر واصفرار الثوب أو توسخه ضعيف لا يعول عليه بخلاف ما لو اعورت الجارية فرابحه على ثمنها فإنه قوي جداً فلم يغتفر .اهـ. قلت: وللبحث فيه مجال فقد يكون تفاوت السعرين أفحش من التفاوت بالعيب والكلام حيث لم يدر المشتري بجميع ذلك فلا فرق يظهر فتدبر .
The book of sale, chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. He said: (and with disclosure of the defect and the defloration of the virgin) meaning he sells on profit with disclosure if the buyer or someone else caused a defect because she (the slave-girl) became intended for destruction so something of the price corresponds to it. Likewise if he had intercourse with her while she is a virgin because the hymen is part of the commodity so something of the price corresponds to it and he has detained her. And it includes when the garment tears by its spreading and folding. And under the first (case) enters when a mouse gnaws the garment or fire burns it, and 'qard' is with qaf and fa'. And 'ta'yib' is the source of 'ayyaba' meaning when he causes a defect in it. And we made absolute in the defect caused by non-buyer, so it includes whether the buyer took the indemnity or not, and whether it was by the buyer's order or not. And what is in al-Hidayah of restriction by his statement 'and the buyer took its indemnity' is coincidental for obligation as in Fath al-Qadir. Then know that Zufar said: He does not sell on profit except with disclosure in both issues, and al-Faqih Abu al-Layth chose it and said: Zufar's statement is better and we adopt it. And he preferred it in Fath al-Qadir. And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) indicated by the first issue that if he finds a defect in the sold item and is pleased with it, he may sell it on profit at the price he bought it for because what is established for him is an option, so dropping it does not prevent selling on profit, as if there was an option of condition or sight. Likewise if he buys it on profit and then discovers fraud and is pleased, he may sell it on profit at what he took it for because what is established for him is merely an option; as in Fath al-Qadir. He said: (and he preferred it in Fath al-Qadir). He said in al-Fath: because the good basis of Murabahah is on absence of fraud, and not mentioning that it decreased is an illusion to the buyer that the mentioned price was for it in complete state, and the norm is that if he knew that the price was for a sound one, he would not take it defective except with a discount. Then he said: but their statement is like the change of price by the command of Allah, so he is not obliged to disclose that he bought it at a time of its high price. Likewise if the garment yellows due to long stay or gets dirty due to strong luster. End quote. He said in al-Nahr: and it may be distinguished that the illusion with price change and yellowing of the garment or its getting dirty is weak not to be relied upon, unlike if the slave-girl becomes one-eyed and he sells on profit at her price, then it is very strong so it is not excused. End quote. I say: there is room for discussion, for the difference between the two prices may be more severe than the difference due to defect, and the speech is when the buyer is unaware of all that, so there is no apparent difference. So reflect.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement 'وببيان بالتعيب ووطء البكر'. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that in a Murabahah sale (sale at cost plus profit), the seller must disclose any defect that occurs to the item after his purchase, whether the defect was caused by the buyer or someone else, because such a defect reduces the value and the buyer deserves a corresponding reduction. Similarly, if the item is a virgin slave-girl and he deflowers her, he must disclose that because the hymen is part of the commodity and its loss reduces value. The disclosure is also required if the garment tears by folding or spreading, or if a mouse gnaws it or fire burns it. The author then discusses the position of Imam Zufar, who held that disclosure is obligatory in both cases, and the preferred view is that it is required. The commentary then addresses a hypothetical: if the buyer discovers a defect and accepts it, he may still sell on profit at the original price, because his acceptance only drops the option, not the right to sell. The deeper discussion concerns whether a change in market price or natural wear (like yellowing) requires disclosure. The commentator distinguishes between a strong illusion (defect) and a weak one (price fluctuation), concluding that defect must be disclosed. This reflects the Hanafi principle of avoiding deception (ghish) in Murabahah, which is built on trust.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale in which the seller discloses the cost price and adds a known profit.
التعيب (al-ta'yib): The act of causing a defect in the commodity.
وطء البكر (wat' al-bikr): Sexual intercourse with a virgin slave-girl, which removes her hymen and diminishes her value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Murabahah sale, if a garment tears due to normal folding and spreading, must the seller disclose this?
A) Yes, because it is a defect that reduces value.
B) No, because normal wear and tear is expected.
C) Only if the buyer asks about the garment's condition.
D) No, because the tear was not caused by a third party.

Key Takeaway
The seller in Murabahah must disclose any defect, no matter the cause, to uphold the trust-based nature of the sale.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Buyer Acceptance and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Disclosure
Commentary on the requirement to disclose defects and defloration in Murabahah sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the effect of buyer acceptance of a defect on Murabahah and compare the views of Abu Hanifa and Zufar on disclosure.

Core Idea
If the buyer accepts a defect, the seller may still sell on profit, but must still disclose per the preferred view of Zufar.

This lesson delves into two interconnected issues: first, what happens when the buyer discovers a defect and accepts it – does that waive disclosure? Second, Imam Zufar's opinion that disclosure is obligatory in all cases, which became the preferred view. Understanding this dispute clarifies the strictness of the Hanafi position on honesty in Murabahah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario of buyer acceptance
The text discusses the case where the buyer finds a defect in the item he purchased and is pleased (رضي به). The seller then wants to sell that item on profit. Does the acceptance remove the need to disclose?
Reference: 'إذا وجد بالمبيع عيباً فرضي به'
2
Understand the legal effect of acceptance
The commentary explains that acceptance only drops the buyer's option (khiyar), not the right to sell on profit. The seller may still sell on profit at the original cost price because the buyer's acceptance means he waived the objection, but the seller must still disclose if reselling on profit because the Murabahah is a fresh contract built on trust.
The text says 'لأن الثابت له خيار، فإسقاطه لا يمنع من البيع مرابحة'.
3
Note the location of the dispute
Imam Zufar (one of the major disciples of Abu Hanifa) held that in both defect and defloration cases, the seller cannot sell on profit without disclosing. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) also require disclosure, but Zufar explicitly states it is obligatory.
The text says 'ثم اعلم أن زفر قال : لا يرابح إلا بالبيان في المسألتين'.
4
Learn the tarjih (preference)
Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth and al-Marghinani (in Fath al-Qadir) preferred Zufar's view and made it the mufta bihi. The reason: Murabahah is built on absence of fraud; non-disclosure creates an illusion that the price was for a sound item.
The text says 'واختاره الفقيه أبو الليث فقال : وقول زفر أجود وبه نأخذ. ورجحه في فتح القدير'.

Checkpoint
According to the preferred Hanafi view (mufta bihi), what is the consequence if the seller sells on profit without disclosing a defect?
A) The sale is valid but the buyer has the option to rescind.
B) The sale is invalid (fasid) because disclosure is a condition.
C) The sale is valid and the buyer has no recourse.
D) The sale is void (batil) from the start.

Distinguishing Defects from Price Fluctuations and Natural Wear
Commentary on the requirement to disclose defects and defloration in Murabahah sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between defects that require disclosure and non-disclosable changes like price fluctuations or natural aging, and evaluate the strength of the illusion.

Core Idea
Price changes and natural wear do not need disclosure in Murabahah, but actual defects do—the key is the strength of the buyer's illusion.

The Murabahah text raises a critical nuance: must the seller disclose that he bought the item at a high price (since market prices change) or that the garment has yellowed over time? The commentary explains that such changes do not require disclosure because the illusion they create is weak. However, a clear defect creates a strong illusion and must be disclosed. This lesson explores this distinction and the ongoing scholarly discussion about it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the general principle
Murabahah is based on trust, so any factor that could mislead the buyer about the true value of the item should be disclosed. The question is: which factors create a significant illusion?
2
Identify the exception for price changes
The text from al-Fath says: change of price by the command of Allah (i.e., market fluctuation) does not need disclosure. Even if the seller bought at a higher price, he is not obliged to mention that when selling on profit at that higher price.
The text: 'كما لو تغير السعر بأمر الله تعالى فإنه لا يجب أن يبين أنه اشتراه في حال غلائه'.
3
Identify the exception for natural wear
The same rule applies to natural changes like yellowing of a garment over time or getting dirty due to strong luster. These are not considered defects that require disclosure.
The text: 'وكذا لو اصفر الثوب لطول مكثه أو توسخ الزام قوي'.
4
Understand the distinction from al-Nahr
Al-Nahr distinguishes these from clear defects: in price change and natural wear, the illusion that the buyer experiences is weak and not reliable, whereas a clear defect like a one-eyed slave-girl creates a strong illusion that cannot be excused.
The text: 'قال في النهر : وقد يفرق بأن الإيهام مع تغير السعر واصفرار الثوب أو توسخه ضعيف لا يعول عليه بخلاف ما لو اعورت الجارية فرابحه على ثمنها فإنه قوي جداً فلم يغتفر'.
5
Evaluate the commentator's reflection
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq questions this distinction, noting that sometimes the price difference can be more extreme than the value reduction from a defect, and if the buyer is unaware of both, there is no apparent difference. This shows a nuanced debate within the school.
The text: 'قلت: وللبحث فيه مجال فقد يكون تفاوت السعرين أفحش من التفاوت بالعيب والكلام حيث لم يدر المشتري بجميع ذلك فلا فرق يظهر فتدبر'.

Source Text

He said: (and with disclosure of the defect and the defloration of the virgin) meaning he sells on profit with disclosure if the buyer or someone else caused a defect because she (the slave-girl) became intended for destruction so something of the price corresponds to it. Likewise if he had intercourse with her while she is a virgin because the hymen is part of the commodity so something of the price corresponds to it and he has detained her. And it includes when the garment tears by its spreading and folding. And under the first (case) enters when a mouse gnaws the garment or fire burns it, and 'qard' is with qaf and fa'. And 'ta'yib' is the source of 'ayyaba' meaning when he causes a defect in it. And we made absolute in the defect caused by non-buyer, so it includes whether the buyer took the indemnity or not, and whether it was by the buyer's order or not. And what is in al-Hidayah of restriction by his statement 'and the buyer took its indemnity' is coincidental for obligation as in Fath al-Qadir. Then know that Zufar said: He does not sell on profit except with disclosure in both issues, and al-Faqih Abu al-Layth chose it and said: Zufar's statement is better and we adopt it. And he preferred it in Fath al-Qadir. And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) indicated by the first issue that if he finds a defect in the sold item and is pleased with it, he may sell it on profit at the price he bought it for because what is established for him is an option, so dropping it does not prevent selling on profit, as if there was an option of condition or sight. Likewise if he buys it on profit and then discovers fraud and is pleased, he may sell it on profit at what he took it for because what is established for him is merely an option; as in Fath al-Qadir. He said: (and he preferred it in Fath al-Qadir). He said in al-Fath: because the good basis of Murabahah is on absence of fraud, and not mentioning that it decreased is an illusion to the buyer that the mentioned price was for it in complete state, and the norm is that if he knew that the price was for a sound one, he would not take it defective except with a discount. Then he said: but their statement is like the change of price by the command of Allah, so he is not obliged to disclose that he bought it at a time of its high price. Likewise if the garment yellows due to long stay or gets dirty due to strong luster. End quote. He said in al-Nahr: and it may be distinguished that the illusion with price change and yellowing of the garment or its getting dirty is weak not to be relied upon, unlike if the slave-girl becomes one-eyed and he sells on profit at her price, then it is very strong so it is not excused. End quote. I say: there is room for discussion, for the difference between the two prices may be more severe than the difference due to defect, and the speech is when the buyer is unaware of all that, so there is no apparent difference. So reflect.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement 'وببيان بالتعيب ووطء البكر'. The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains that in a Murabahah sale (sale at cost plus profit), the seller must disclose any defect that occurs to the item after his purchase, whether the defect was caused by the buyer or someone else, because such a defect reduces the value and the buyer deserves a corresponding reduction. Similarly, if the item is a virgin slave-girl and he deflowers her, he must disclose that because the hymen is part of the commodity and its loss reduces value. The disclosure is also required if the garment tears by folding or spreading, or if a mouse gnaws it or fire burns it. The author then discusses the position of Imam Zufar, who held that disclosure is obligatory in both cases, and the preferred view is that it is required. The commentary then addresses a hypothetical: if the buyer discovers a defect and accepts it, he may still sell on profit at the original price, because his acceptance only drops the option, not the right to sell. The deeper discussion concerns whether a change in market price or natural wear (like yellowing) requires disclosure. The commentator distinguishes between a strong illusion (defect) and a weak one (price fluctuation), concluding that defect must be disclosed. This reflects the Hanafi principle of avoiding deception (ghish) in Murabahah, which is built on trust.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale in which the seller discloses the cost price and adds a known profit.
التعيب (al-ta'yib): The act of causing a defect in the commodity.
وطء البكر (wat' al-bikr): Sexual intercourse with a virgin slave-girl, which removes her hymen and diminishes her value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which of the following does NOT require disclosure in a Murabahah sale?
A) The seller bought the item at a time of high market price.
B) The item has a tear caused by a mouse.
C) The seller deflowers a virgin slave-girl.
D) The garment is stained due to a fire.

Key Takeaway
In Murabahah, only defects that diminish the intrinsic value of the commodity require disclosure; external factors like price fluctuations and natural wear do not.

Ruling on Tawliyah with Unknown Cost and Option in Session
Commentary on two matn statements about Tawliyah sale with unknown cost and option if knowledge gained in session

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling (fasid) for a Tawliyah sale with unknown cost, identify the buyer's option to ratify within the session, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the possibility of validity.

Core Idea
A sale with unknown cost is fasid but can be saved if the buyer learns the price in the session.

In this lesson, we examine a key ruling from Hanafi sales law: a Tawliyah (cost-transfer) sale where the buyer does not know the seller's original cost. The passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq explains why the sale is initially fasid (voidable) and how the buyer's option within the session can validate it. We will also see an intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether such a contract carries the possibility of validity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of Tawliyah sale
Tawliyah is a contract where the seller transfers the item at exactly the cost he paid, without profit. The buyer must know this cost for the contract to be sound. Identify the contract type and its key condition.
Tawliyah is distinct from Murabahah (profit sale) and Musawamah (negotiated price).
2
Identify the cause of invalidity
If the buyer does not know the cost price, the sale is fasid (voidable) due to jahalat al-thaman (ignorance of the price). This is a defect that prevents the contract from being fully valid, but it is not void ab initio.
Ignorance of an essential term (price) renders the contract fasid in Hanafi law.
3
Analyze the option in the session
If the buyer learns the cost price while still in the same session (majlis) as the contract, he has an option to either accept or reject. The fasd is not yet settled, so the contract can be ratified by his acceptance in the session.
The option is restricted to the session; after parting, the invalidity becomes final.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
There is a difference within the school: the correct view holds that such a contract is fasid with the possibility of validity (عرضية الصحة), meaning it can become valid if the option is exercised. Another transmitted view says it is absolutely fasid (عرضية الفساد) and cannot be rectified.
The practical difference: according to the correct view, engaging in this contract is prohibited (haram); according to the weak view, it is not.
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider a buyer who discovers the cost after leaving the session. According to the correct Hanafi position, the sale remains fasid and cannot be ratified. The buyer has no option and must cancel the contract if he wishes.
This highlights the importance of timely disclosure of the cost.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو ولى رجلاً شيئاً بما قام عليه ولم يعلم المشتري بكم قام عليه فسد) أي البيع لجهالة الثمن وكذا لو ولاه بما اشتراه والمرابحة فيهما كالتولية . قوله : (ولو علم في المجلس خير) أي بين أخذه وتركه لأن الفساد لم يتقرر فإذا حصل العلم في المجلس جعل كابتداء العقد وصار كتأخير القبول إلى آخر المجلس. قيد بالمجلس لأنه بعد الافتراق عنه يتقرر الفساد فلا يقبل الإصلاح، ونظيره بيع الشيء برقمه إذا علم في المجلس وإنما يتخير لأن الرضاً لم يتم قبله لعدم لعلم فيتخير كما في خيار الرؤية. وظاهر كلام المصنف وغيره أن هذا العقد ينعقد فاسداً بعرضية الصحة وهو الصحيح خلافاً للمروي عرضية الفساد ؛ كذا له في فتح القدير. وينبغي أن تظهر ثمرة الاختلاف في حرمة مباشرته فعل الصحيح يحرم وعلى الضعيف لا والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم.
He said: (And if he appoints someone as partner in something at the price it cost him, and the buyer does not know at how much it cost him, the sale is void) meaning the sale is void due to ignorance of the price. Likewise if he appoints him at what he bought it for, and Murabahah in both is like Tawliyah. He said: (And if he knows in the session, he has an option) meaning between taking it and leaving it, because the invalidity is not yet settled. So if knowledge is obtained in the session, it is treated like a new contract and becomes like delaying acceptance until the end of the session. It is restricted to the session because after parting it becomes settled invalidity and cannot be rectified. Its analogy is the sale of an item at its price marked (bi-raqmihi) if he knows in the session. He is given an option because satisfaction was not completed before due to lack of knowledge, so he has an option as in the option of sight. The apparent meaning of the author and others is that this contract is concluded as void (fasid) with the possibility of validity, and this is the correct view contrary to the transmitted view of absolute invalidity. As in Fath al-Qadir. And the consequence of the difference should appear in the prohibition of engaging in it: according to the sound view it is prohibited, according to the weak view it is not. And Allah Most High knows best.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage comments on two matn statements in the chapter of Murabahah and Tawliyah. The first ruling: If a person makes a Tawliyah sale (selling at the same price he paid) without informing the buyer of the actual cost price, the sale is fasid (voidable) due to ignorance of the price. The same applies to a sale in which the seller says 'I sell it to you at what I bought it for' (Murabahah but without profit? Actually the text says 'Murabahah in both is like Tawliyah' meaning the same ruling applies to profit sales). The second ruling: If the buyer learns the cost price while still in the session of the contract, he has an option to either accept or reject the sale. This is because the invalidity (fasād) does not become final until after the session ends. The analogy is made to the sale of an item at its marked price (bi-raqmihi) where the buyer has an option if he discovers the price in the session. The commentary also notes an intra-Hanafi dispute: whether such a contract is fasid with the possibility of becoming valid (the correct view) or absolutely fasid. The practical difference is whether engaging in such a contract is prohibited (haram) or not. The relied-upon view is that it is fasid with the possibility of validity, so it is prohibited to engage in it.

Key Terms
التولية (al-tawliyah): A sale in which the seller transfers the item at exactly the cost he paid, without profit.
جهالة الثمن (jahalat al-thaman): Ignorance of the price, which renders a sale fasid in Hanafi law.
خيار المجلس (khiyar al-majlis): The option to rescind the contract within the session, as applied to this case of learning the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a Tawliyah sale where the buyer does not know the cost price, what happens if he learns the cost after leaving the session?
A) He may still exercise the option to accept or reject.
B) The sale becomes valid automatically.
C) The sale is void and cannot be ratified.
D) He must pay the cost price based on market value.

Key Takeaway
A Tawliyah sale with unknown cost is initially fasid, but the buyer can ratify it by learning the cost within the same session, after which the invalidity becomes final.

The Effect of Excessive Ghabn in Sales: The Hanafi Internal Dispute
Defines ghabn and presents multiple narrations on whether to allow return due to excessive ghabn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the Zahir al-Riwayah position and later fatwa on ghabn fahish, and identify when a return is allowed according to each view.

Core Idea
Gross deception alone does not void a sale in the main Hanafi view, but later fatwas allow return for public ease.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq defines ghabn (deception) and presents a spectrum of Hanafi opinions on whether excessive ghabn gives a right to rescind a sale. The Zahir al-Riwayah holds that the sale remains binding, while later scholars permitted rescission under certain circumstances, especially when there is actual deception (taghrir). Understanding this dispute is crucial for applying the correct ruling in modern transactions.

Guided Path
1
Define ghabn
Identify the linguistic meaning of ghabn as a deficiency in price or value, and note the term ghabn fahish for gross deception.
2
State the Zahir al-Riwayah position
According to the transmitted ruling from Imam Muhammad's six books, the buyer has no right to return the item or reclaim the price solely due to excessive ghabn.
This is the foundational Hanafi ruling.
3
Identify the opposing position from later scholars
Some later Hanafi authorities, such as Abu Bakr al-Zaranjari and al-Qadi al-Jalal, allowed rescission, citing public interest and the principle of removing hardship.
This is considered a fatwa position.
4
Analyze the specific scenarios
Work through the examples given: when the seller deceives (taghrir), when the broker deceives, when the seller misstates the value, etc. Note that in cases of actual deception, the right to return is more widely accepted.
The presence of taghrir changes the ruling.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Although the madhhab's original ruling is no return, the author notes that many later scholars gave fatwa allowing return. In practice, the mufta bihi may be the opinion that facilitates dealings, especially when there is deception.
This illustrates the concept of tatabbu' al-rukhas (following dispensations).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في البيع والشراء غبناً من باب ضرب مثل غبنه فانغبن وغبنه أي نقصه، وغبن بالبناء للمفعول فهو مغبون أي منقوص في الثمن أو غيره والغبينة اسم منه اهـ. وفي القنية : من اشترى شيئاً وغبن فيه غبناً فاحشاً فله أن يرده على البائع بحكم الغبن وفيه روايتان، ويفتى بالرد رفقاً بالناس. ثم رقم لآخر : وقع البيع بغبن فاحش ذكر الجصاص وهو أبو بكر الرازي في واقعاته أن للمشتري أن يرد وللبائع أن يسترد ؛ وهو اختيار أبي بكر الزرنجري والقاضي الجلال، وأكثر روايات كتاب المضاربة الرد بالغبن الفاحش وبه يفتى. ثم رقم لآخر : ليس له الرد والاسترداد وهو جواب ظاهر الرواية وبه أفتى بعضهم. ثم رقم لآخر: إن غر المشتري البائع فله أن يسترد، وكذا إن غر البائع المشتري له أن يرد ، ثم رقم لآخر : قال البائع للمشتري قيمته كذا فاشتراه ثم ظهر أنها أقل فله الرد وإن لم يقل فلا، وبه أفتى صدر الإسلام. ثم رقم لآخر ولو لم يغره البائع ولكن غره الدلال فله الرد، ولو اشترى فيلق إلابريسم خارج البلد ممن لم يكن عالماً بسعر البلد بغبن فاحش فللبائع أن يرجع على المشتري بالفيلق مثله في حق المشتري قال لغزال لا معرفة لي بالغزل فأتني بغزل اشتريه فأتى رجل بغزل لهذا الغزال ولم يعلم به المشتري فجعل نفسه دلالاً بينهما واشترى ذلك الغزل له بأزيد من ثمن المثل وصرف المشتري بعضه إلى حاجته، ثم علم بالغين وبما صنع فله أن يرد الباقي بحصته من الثمن. قال رضي الله تعالى عنه : والصواب أن يرد الباقي ومثل ما صرف إلى حاجته وليسترد جميع الثمن كمن اشترى بيتاً مملوءاً من بر فإذا فيه دكان عظيم فله الرد وأخذ جميع الثمن قبل إنفاق شيء من عينه وبعده يرد الباقي ومثل ما أنفق ويسترد الثمن؛ كذا ذكره أبو يوسف ومحمد اهـ فقد تحرر أن المذهب عدم الرد بغبن فاحش ولكن بعض مشايخنا أفتى بالرد به.
In buying and selling, ghabn (deception) is from the chapter of darb, like 'ghabanahu fa-nghabana' meaning he cheated him, and 'ghubina' in passive means he was cheated, i.e., diminished in price or otherwise, and 'ghabinah' is the noun. In al-Qunyah: Whoever buys something and is cheated with a gross deception, he may return it to the seller by the ruling of ghabn, and there are two narrations, and fatwa is given for return out of kindness to people. Then he numbered another: The sale occurred with gross deception; al-Jassas, Abu Bakr al-Razi, mentioned in his Waqi'at that the buyer may return and the seller may reclaim; and this is the choice of Abu Bakr al-Zaranjari and al-Qadi al-Jalal, and most narrations of the book of Mudarabah adopt return for gross deception and fatwa is given accordingly. Then he numbered another: He does not have the right to return or reclaim, and this is the answer of Zahir al-Riwayah, and some gave fatwa with it. Then he numbered another: If the buyer deceived the seller, he may reclaim; likewise if the seller deceived the buyer, he may return. Then he numbered another: The seller told the buyer 'its value is such' and he bought it, then it appeared less, he may return; if he did not say that, he may not; and Sadr al-Islam gave fatwa with this. Then he numbered another: Even if the seller did not deceive him but the broker deceived him, he may return. And if he bought a bundle of silk outside the city from someone who was not aware of the city's price with gross deception, the seller may claim from the buyer the like of the bundle in respect of the buyer's right. He said to a spinner: 'I have no knowledge of spinning; bring me spinning to buy.' A man brought spinning for this spinner, and the buyer did not know about it, so he made himself a broker between them and bought that spinning for him at more than the fair price, and the buyer used some of it for his need. Then he learned of the deception and what he did; he may return the remainder for its share of the price. The author said: The correct is that he returns the remainder and the like of what he used for his need and takes back all the price, like one who buys a house full of wheat and finds in it a big shop; he may return and take all the price before spending anything of its essence, and after spending he returns the remainder and the like of what he spent and takes back the price; as mentioned by Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. So it is established that the madhhab is non-return for gross deception, but some of our shaykhs gave fatwa for return.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the legal effect of gross deception (ghabn fahish) in a sale. The author begins by defining ghabn as a deficiency in price or otherwise. He then cites various narrations from later authorities. The ruling in the Zahir al-Riwayah, attributed to Abu Hanifa and the two disciples, is that excessive ghabn does not give the buyer the right to rescind the contract. However, later scholars like Abu Bakr al-Zaranjari and al-Qadi al-Jalal allowed rescission, and fatwa was given for it out of public interest. The author reconciles by stating the madhhab's original position but notes that some gave fatwa with return. The passage includes several scenarios: when the seller deceives, when the broker deceives, and when both parties are unaware of the true value. The author concludes with a ruling from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad about returning the remainder and the equivalent of what was consumed. The dominant ruling remains non-return, but the fatwa of return is known as a dispensation.

Key Terms
غبن (ghabn): Deficiency in price or value, whether in the transaction or elsewhere, resulting from concealment or misrepresentation.
غبن فاحش (ghabn fahish): Gross deception where the difference between the actual value and the paid price is significant, typically not tolerated by custom.
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The transmitted ruling from Imam Muhammad's six books, which is the primary source of Hanafi law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah, what is the ruling on a buyer who suffers excessive ghabn (gross deception) in a sale?
A) The buyer may return the item and get a full refund.
B) The buyer may only return if the seller explicitly guaranteed the price.
C) The buyer has no right to return the item solely due to excessive ghabn.
D) The buyer may deduct the difference from the price.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, excessive ghabn alone does not give a right to rescind a sale, but later fatwas permit return when there is active deception (taghrir) to ease dealings.

Defining Mithli (Fungible) Goods in Hanafi Law
Defines mithli goods and provides examples from Hanafi authorities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of mithli goods, identify the condition of uniformity (taqarrub), and classify common items as mithli or qimi.

Core Idea
Not all weighed or measured goods are mithli — only those sufficiently uniform to have exact substitutes.

The classification of goods as mithli (fungible) or qimi (unique) is foundational in Hanafi law for guarantees, sales, and damages. In this passage, the commentator clarifies the precise definition of mithli and provides authoritative lists to resolve common confusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criteria
Read the passage and note the general rule: items measured by weight (mawzun) or volume (makil) are candidates for mithli. But not all are automatically mithli.
2
Apply the condition of uniformity
Understand that only items whose parts are uniform (mutakarih) and do not vary significantly are mithli. Variant items (mutafawwit) are qimi.
Sadr al-Islam explicitly refines the general rule.
3
Recognize examples from authorities
Study the lists from various shaykhs and from Jami' al-Fusulayn: vinegar, juice, flour, cotton, wool, etc. are mithli. Note that manufactured goods are excluded unless uniform.
4
Apply to the weaver scenario
Examine the case: a weaver denies thread, then returns it woven. The ruling that he must replace with like thread (because it is mithli) shows the legal consequence.
Frames the practical effect of the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومثل ما صرف إلى حاجته مقتضاه أن الغزل مثلي لأنه موزون لا قيمي ويدل عليه ما يأتي في الربا حيث عدوه من الأموال الربوية ورأيت بخط بعض مشايخ مشايخنا ما نصه : كل ما يكال أو یوزن وليس في تبعيضه مضرة يعني غير المصنوع فهو مثلي، وكذا العددي المتقارب كالجوز والبيض والفلوس ونحوها. وذكر صدر الإسلام أبو اليسر رحمه الله تعالى في شرح كتاب الغصب : ليس كل مكيل مثلياً ولا كل موزون إنما المثلي من المكيلات والموزونات ما متقاربة، أما ما متفاوت فليس بمثلي فكانت المكيلات والموزونات والعدديات سواء عمادية من أنواع الضمانات اهـ . قلت : ورأيت في الفصل الثالث والثلاثين من جامع الفصولين برمز (فر) الخل والعصير والدقيق والنخالة والجص والنورة والقطن والصوف وغزله والتبن بجميع أنواعه مثلي. ثم ذكر بعده بنحو كراسة في هذا الفصل في ضمان النساج دفع إليه غزلاً لينسج فجحد الحائك الغزل وحلف ثم أقر وجاء به منسوجاً فلو نسجه قبل جحوده فله أجره، ولو نسجه بعد جحوده ضمن غزلاً مثله لأنه مثلي ولا أجر له الخ. فهذا صريح النقل والله الحمد فاندفع قول من قال إنه قيمي فتنبه
His statement: 'and the like of what he spent for his need' implies that spinning is mithli because it is weighed, not a commodity of value, and this is indicated by what comes in Riba where they count it among usurious goods. And I saw in the handwriting of some of our shaykhs' shaykhs the text: 'Everything that is measured by volume or weight, and in dividing it there is no harm — meaning it is unmanufactured — is mithli. Likewise, countable items that are similar, like walnuts, eggs, coins, and the like.' Sadr al-Islam Abu al-Yusr mentioned in his commentary on the book of Ghasp: 'Not every measured (makil) or weighed (mawzun) item is mithli; rather, mithli from measured and weighed items is what is close (mutakarih). As for what is variant (mutafawwit), it is not mithli. Thus measured, weighed, and countable items are all equal in being of the types of guarantees.' He said: And I saw in the thirty-third chapter of Jami' al-Fusulayn with the code (far): vinegar, juice, flour, bran, plaster, lime, cotton, wool, its spinning, straw in all its types are mithli. Then he mentioned after that about a page in this chapter regarding the guarantee of the weaver: someone gave him thread to weave; the weaver denied the thread and swore, then confessed and brought it woven. If he wove it before his denial, he has his wage; if he wove it after his denial, he guarantees a like thread because it is mithli, and he has no wage, etc. This is explicit narration, praise be to Allah, so it refutes the statement of whoever said it is a commodity of value (qimi). Be aware.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of mithli (fungible) goods in Hanafi law, which are essential for understanding guarantees and damages. The commentator begins by explaining that the author's phrase 'and the like of what he spent' indicates that thread is mithli because it is weighed and not a unique item. He then quotes a note from earlier scholars that anything measured by weight or volume that can be divided without harm (i.e., unmanufactured natural substances) is mithli. Similarly, countable items that are uniform like walnuts and eggs are mithli. Sadr al-Islam Abu al-Yusr clarifies that not all weighed or measured items are mithli; only those that are relatively uniform. Items that vary significantly are qimi (unique). He then cites a list from Jami' al-Fusulayn of items that are mithli: vinegar, juice, flour, bran, plaster, lime, cotton, wool, spun yarn, and straw in all types. The passage concludes with an example of a weaver who received thread to weave but denied it and then returned it woven; if he wove it after denial, he must replace it with like thread because it is mithli. This reinforces the classification.

Key Terms
مثلي (mithli): Goods that are fungible; they have substitutes of the same kind and quality, typically determined by weight, measure, or count.
قيمي (qimi): Goods that are unique and have no exact substitute, like real estate or custom-made items; their value is assessed by market price.
متقارب (mutakarih): Similar and close in nature, not varying significantly—a condition for an item to be classified as mithli.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the list from Jami' al-Fusulayn, which of the following is NOT considered mithli?
A) Vinegar
B) Wool
C) A custom-made rug
D) Flour

Key Takeaway
Mithli goods are those that are uniform in weight, measure, or count, making them replaceable in kind; all others are qimi and compensated by market value.

Selling Real Estate Before Taking Possession
Presents the ruling on selling real estate before possession and the disagreement between the imams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling real estate before possession, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the exception for risky property.

Core Idea
Selling real estate before possession is valid unless the property is prone to destruction.

This lesson explores a key intra-Hanafi dispute: can you sell real estate before physically taking possession? Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say yes, while Muhammad says no. The relied-upon fatwa follows the majority, but with important qualifications when the property is at risk. Understanding this ruling clarifies the role of gharar and the scope of the prophetic prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
What is the act under discussion? Selling real estate before the buyer has taken possession (qabd). According to the author, the default ruling is 'sahih' (valid).
2
State the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the sale valid. Muhammad says it is invalid. Note that this is a disagreement within the same madhhab.
3
Analyze each side's dalil
Muhammad relies on the hadith 'Do not sell what you have not taken possession of' and analogy to movables and lease. The majority argue that the contract's pillars are complete, gharar is negligible because real estate rarely perishes, and the hadith is interpreted in light of other proofs.
4
Identify the tarjih and mufta bihi
The fatwa is given according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, as stated in al-Kafi and al-Fawa'id al-Zahiriyyah. This is the operative position.
5
Note the qualifications
The ruling is not absolute. If the property is in a location prone to destruction (e.g., on a seashore, affected by sand, or an upper floor at risk), it is treated like movables and sale before possession is invalid. This shows the ruling is conditioned on safety.

Section on dealing with the sold item and the price before taking possession, and the increase and decrease in them and deferment of debts. His statement: (Sale of real estate before taking possession is valid) i.e., according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: It is not permitted due to the absolute nature of the hadith which prohibits selling what has not been received, and by analogy to movables and to lease. Their proof is that the pillar of sale has been issued by its people in its place, and there is no gharar (deception) because destruction of real estate is rare contrary to movables, and the prohibited gharar is the gharar of contract rescission, and the hadith is negated due to acting on the proofs of permissibility and lease. In al-Khaniyyah: If he bought land with tender vegetables and handed it to the seller for partnership (mu'amalah) on halves before taking possession, it is not allowed because he leased the land. Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of selling real estate before the buyer takes physical possession (qabd). The Hanafi imams differ: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold it valid, while Muhammad says it is invalid based on the hadith 'Do not sell what you have not taken possession of' and analogy to movables and lease. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) argue that the contract's pillars are complete, there is no significant gharar because real estate rarely perishes, and the hadith is interpreted in light of other proofs allowing it. The fatwa is according to their view. However, there are qualifications: if the real estate is in a location where it is prone to destruction (like a beach or sand dunes), it is treated like movables and cannot be sold before possession. Also, if the sale includes crops, it is invalid because it involves selling something before possession. The commentator notes that the author used the term 'sahih' (valid) rather than 'nafidh' (effective) or 'lazim' (binding) to indicate that the contract is essentially sound but might be subject to rescission if the buyer fails to take possession.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession of the purchased item, which is a condition for selling it further.
غرر (gharar): Uncertainty or risk in a contract that could lead to dispute.
معاملة (mu'amalah): A partnership arrangement where one party provides land and the other labor and seeds, sharing the produce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Invalid, due to the hadith.
B) Valid, according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.
C) Valid only if the buyer has seen it.
D) Invalid if the seller is not trustworthy.

Key Takeaway
The default Hanafi ruling is that selling real estate before possession is valid, but this is conditioned on the property being safe from destruction.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Selling Real Estate and Movable Property Before Possession: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Proofs
Commentary on the ruling of selling before possession with intra-Hanafi dispute and proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling real estate before possession, explain the intra-Hanafi dispute, and apply the distinction between real estate and movable property.

Core Idea
Selling real estate before possession is valid; selling movable property before possession is invalid — due to rarity vs frequency of destruction.

This lesson examines a key commercial ruling where the Hanafi school distinguishes between real estate and movable property regarding sale before possession. The passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with their proofs. We'll work through the legal reasoning, including the interpretation of the Prophetic hadith and the role of gharar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage explicitly discusses عقار (real estate) and منقول (movable property). State the initial ruling for each: real estate is valid to sell before possession; movable property is not.
The ruling on movable property is agreed upon; the dispute is only on real estate.
2
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Note that Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold real estate sales before possession as valid, while Imam Muhammad holds it invalid. Identify the basis for each.
Imam Muhammad analogizes real estate to movable property.
3
Examine the proofs
Identify the hadith (النهي عن بيع ما لم يقبض) and the arguments of gharar and qiyas. Understand how each imam uses these proofs.
The hadith is the primary textual evidence; the concept of gharar distinguishes the cases.
4
Analyze the specification of the hadith
Explain how Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf specify the hadith to movable property only, based on the rarity of destruction in real estate. Understand the principle that gharar that leads to contract rescission is the prohibited kind.
This is a classic example of takhsis (specification) based on illa.
5
Apply the exceptions
Note even real estate cannot be sold before possession if it is in a location where destruction is likely (e.g., seashore, sand dunes). This confirms the underlying rationale of gharar.
These exceptions show the ruling is not arbitrary but tied to the presence or absence of risk.

Chapter on explaining disposal of the sold item and price before taking possession, increase and decrease therein, and deferment of debts. If the seller disposes of it before his possession, it is on two types: either by the buyer's order or without his order. Likewise, if he ordered the seller to hire him out to so-and-so specified or unspecified, he did, it is valid and the lessee becomes possessor for the buyer first then becomes possessor for himself, and the rent that the seller takes from the lessee he counts it from the price if it is of its type. Likewise, if the seller lent the slave or after without the seller's permission, the pronoun refers to possession and the prepositional phrase is attached to it or a deleted state from it, i.e., possession that occurs without the seller's permission is like non-possession because the seller can take it back to detain it for the price. His statement: (and reconciliation because it is sale) meaning reconciliation regarding debt as in al-Fath, and the expression of al-Nahr by khul' is a slip of the pen if not from distortion of copyists. His statement: (and the principle as in al-Idah) etc., this principle does not apply according to Muhammad, so apparently...
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the ruling of selling before taking possession. It addresses both real estate (عقار) and movable property (منقول). The passage begins by stating the ruling: selling real estate before possession is valid according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf, while Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds it is not permissible. The key proof for the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) is that the pillar of sale (ijab and qabul) is complete, and there is no gharar (uncertainty) because destruction of real estate is rare. The hadith prohibiting selling what one has not yet received (النهي عن بيع ما لم يقبض) is interpreted by them as applying to movable property only, and the gharar that is prohibited is the gharar of contract rescission, which is not present in real estate. Imam Muhammad analogizes real estate to movable property and hire. The commentary also discusses special cases: if the real estate is in a location where destruction is likely (e.g., seashore or near sand dunes), then its sale before possession is not permissible even according to Abu Hanifa. For movable property, the ruling is that sale before possession is invalid due to the explicit prohibition and the presence of gharar. However, other transfers like gift, charity, and loan of movable property before possession are permissible according to Muhammad (the most sound view), contrary to Abu Yusuf. The passage also covers related matters: Kitabah (contractual manumission) of a slave before possession is suspended; marriage of a slave girl before possession is valid despite gharar; will before possession is valid by consensus. The principle stated is: any consideration owned under a contract that is rescinded by its destruction before possession cannot be disposed of before possession. The passage ends with a discussion of the seller's disposal of the sold item before possession, which is valid if done by the buyer's order, and the buyer becomes a possessor of the item through the seller's act.

Key Terms
الغرر (al-gharar): Uncertainty or risk in a contract that may lead to dispute; specifically the risk of contract rescission due to destruction of the subject matter before delivery.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical or constructive possession of the sold item. For real estate, possession includes taking control and removing obstacles; for movable property, it requires physical taking or transfer to a designated place.
بيع ما لم يقبض (bay' ma lam yuqbad): Sale of an item before the seller has delivered it to the buyer. The hadith prohibits this for movable property, but for real estate it is permissible according to the majority of Hanafis.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling on selling a piece of land that has been purchased but not yet physically taken possession of?
A) It is invalid because the hadith prohibits selling what has not been received.
B) It is valid according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and this is the position for fatwa.
C) It is valid only if the land has no crops.
D) It is valid but requires the seller's permission.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling real estate before possession due to rarity of destruction, while prohibiting it for movable property to avoid gharar.

Selling Movables Before Qabd: Ruling and Exceptions
Commentary explaining the ruling and exceptions with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling movable property before taking possession and identify which transactions are permissible exceptions according to the relied-upon view.

Core Idea
Selling before qabd is fasid, but gifts and other non-sale contracts are permissible; the test is whether the contract is annulled by destruction.

Have you ever bought something online and wondered if you could resell it before it arrives? The Hanafi school has a clear ruling: selling a movable item before taking physical or constructive possession (qabd) is invalid due to the Prophet's prohibition and the risk (gharar) it entails. However, not every transfer is a sale. This lesson unpacks the general rule, its proof, the crucial exceptions, and an intra-madhhab difference between Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Ruling and Its Dalil
Locate the commentator's opening statement that a sale of movable property before qabd is invalid (la yasihh). Identify the two proofs cited: the hadith prohibition and the presence of gharar (risk of rescission upon destruction).
The dalil is a hadith of the Prophet (saw) prohibiting selling what one has not taken possession of.
2
Understand the Exceptions Mentioned
Read the list of transactions that are NOT prohibited before qabd: gift, charity, loan, kitabah (emancipation contract), marriage of a slave girl, and bequest. Note that these are permissible because they are not sales or are held not to be annulled by destruction.
For kitabah, it is suspended until the seller's lien is resolved; marriage is valid despite gharar by analogy to a runaway slave.
3
Distinguish the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Observe that Abu Yusuf held gift, charity, and loan before qabd to be impermissible, while Imam Muhammad considered them permissible and this is the relied-upon view. Also note that on marriage nullification upon rescission of the sale, Abu Yusuf's view (that the marriage dissolves) is the chosen view.
The text uses 'asahh' (more correct) for Muhammad on gifts, and 'mukhtar' (chosen) for Abu Yusuf on marriage.
4
Apply the Governing Principle
The commentator concludes with a principle from al-Idah: any consideration owned by a contract that is annulled by its destruction before qabd cannot be disposed of; otherwise, it can. Use this to test whether a new transaction (e.g., leasing) falls under the prohibition.
Leasing is included because it is a sale of usufructs; the hire for a specific item is also prohibited before qabd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا) بيع المنقول) أي لا يصح لنهيه عن بيع ما لم يقبض ولأن فيه غرر انفساخ العقد على اعتبار الهلاك قيد بالبيع لأن هبته والتصدق به وإقراضه من غير البائع جائز عند محمد وهو الأصح خلافاً لأبي يوسف وأما كتابة العبد المبيع قبل القبض موقوفة وللبائع حبسه بالثمن وإن نقده نفذت؛ كذا ذكر الشارح ولا خصوصية لها بل كل عقد يقبل النقض فهو موقوف كما قدمناه وأما تزويج الجارية المبيعة قبل قبضها فجائز لأن الغرر لا يمنع جوازه بدليل صحة تزويج الآبق . وأما الوصية به قبل القبض فصحيحة اتفاقاً لأنها أخت الميراث، ولو زوجها قبل القبض ثم فسخ البيع انفسخ النكاح على قول أبي يوسف وهو المختار كما في الولوالجية. وأطلق البيع فشمل الإجارة لأنها بيع المنافع والصلح لأنه بيع قالوا: ما لا يجوز بيعه قبل القبض لا تجوز إجارته، ولا يجوز بيع الأجرة العين قبل القبض لأنها بمنزلة المبيع. وأراد بالمنقول المبيع المنقول فجاز بيع غيره كالمهر وبدل الخلع وبدل الصلح على دم العمد والأصل كما في الإيضاح أن كل عوض ملك بعقد ينفسخ بهلاكه قبل قبضه فالتصرف فيه غير جائز وما لا فجائز
His saying: '(No) sale of movable property' means it is not valid due to the prohibition of selling what has not been taken possession, and because there is a risk (gharar) of the contract being dissolved upon the consideration of destruction. He restricted it to sale because giving it as a gift, charity, or loan from other than the seller is permissible according to Muhammad, and it is the more correct [view], contrary to Abu Yusuf. As for the kitabah (writing of emancipation) of a slave that has been sold before taking possession, it is suspended, and the seller has the right to detain him for the price, and if he has paid the price, it is executed. Thus the commentator mentioned, and there is no exclusivity to it; rather, every contract that can be rescinded is suspended, as we have presented. As for marrying off a slave girl that has been sold before taking possession, it is permissible because gharar does not prevent its validity, as evidenced by the validity of marrying a runaway slave. As for bequeathing it (the sold item) before taking possession, it is valid by agreement because it is akin to inheritance. And if he married her before taking possession and then the sale was rescinded, the marriage is dissolved according to Abu Yusuf, and that is the chosen view as in al-Wilwaljiah. And he (the author) made the ruling general [by saying 'sale'], so it includes leasing because it is sale of usufructs, and sulh (compromise) because it is sale. They said: Whatever is not permissible to sell before taking possession, its leasing is also not permissible, and it is not permissible to sell the hire for a specific [item] before taking possession because it is like the sold item. And he meant by 'movable' the sold movable property, so the sale of other things is permissible, such as the dower (mahr), the consideration for khul', and the consideration for sulh on deliberate homicide. And the principle, as in al-Idah, is that every consideration which is owned by a contract that is annulled by its destruction before taking possession, the disposal of it is not permissible, and what is not [so] is permissible.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn statement 'لا بيع المنقول' (No sale of movable property before qabd). The commentator clarifies that the prohibition applies specifically to sale, not to all transactions. Citing a hadith prohibition, he explains the invalidity due to gharar (risk). He then lists exceptions: gift, charity, and loan are permissible according to Imam Muhammad (the relied-upon view), contra Abu Yusuf. Kitabah (emancipation contract) is suspended until the seller's lien is satisfied. Marriage of a slave girl before qabd is valid despite gharar, analogous to marrying a runaway slave. Will is valid by consensus. Leasing and sulh are included in the prohibition because they are types of sale. The principle from al-Idah is stated: any consideration owned by a contract that is annulled by its destruction before qabd cannot be disposed of; otherwise, it can.

Key Terms
غرر (gharar): Risk or uncertainty that can lead to cancellation of the contract.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical or constructive possession of the purchased item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, which of the following transactions by the buyer of a movable item before taking possession is permissible?
A) Selling it back to the seller
B) Giving it as a gift to a third party
C) Leasing it to someone
D) Pawning it as collateral

Key Takeaway
Selling movable property before qabd is fasid, but gratuitous transfers like gifts are exempted; the test is whether the transaction is a sale or equivalent that would be annulled by destruction before qabd.

Gift as Iqalah: Rescinding a Sale Before Possession
Commentary on the rulings when the buyer deals with the original seller before qabd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a sale and a gift to the original seller before qabd, stating the ruling for each and explaining why gift functions as iqalah.

Core Idea
Gifting back to the seller before qabd rescinds the sale; selling back does not — the legal form determines the effect.

This passage addresses a specific scenario in sales: what happens if the buyer deals with the original seller before taking possession? The matn's general prohibition is refined: a sale back is invalid and leaves the original contract intact, but a gift accepted by the seller is treated as an iqalah (mutual rescission), annulling the first sale. This distinction rests on the Hanafi view that a gift to the seller in this context is a metaphor for rescission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The matn generally prohibits the buyer from disposing of the item before qabd. This passage applies that to selling back to the seller: such a sale is invalid (faasid) and does not annul the first sale.
2
Contrast with the gift scenario
If the buyer gifts the item to the seller and the seller accepts, this is treated as iqalah (rescission), so the first sale is annulled. The key difference is that the gift is a metaphor (majaz) for iqalah in Hanafi fiqh.
The gift must be of the same item (the subject of the sale) and accepted by the seller.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning
Why is a gift a metaphor for iqalah? Because iqalah means returning the item to the seller in exchange for annulment, and a gift achieves the same end. A sale, however, implies a new transaction, not a simple return.
The Hanafi school relies on linguistic analogy and custom (urf) for this interpretation.
4
Apply the example from al-Khaniyya
The passage cites al-Khaniyya: if after iqalah but before repossession the buyer buys the item again from the seller, the purchase is valid. But if the seller sells it to a third party after iqalah, that sale is invalid because the seller does not have possession.
This shows the effects of iqalah: the item reverts to the seller's ownership but not possession, so the seller cannot transfer it to another until physical repossession.
5
Conclude the principle
The buyer's disposal before qabd is generally prohibited, except when it takes the form of a gift accepted as iqalah. The legal form (sale vs. gift) determines whether the first contract is rescinded.
This principle applies to all sales, not just slaves or specific items.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأطلق في منع البيع فشمل ما إذا باعه من بائعه قبل القبض لم يصح ولا ينتقض البيع الأول بخلاف ما إذا وهبه منه وقبلها فإنه ينتقض لأن الهبة مجاز عن الإقالة بخلاف البيع. وفي الخانية : اشترى عبداً وقبضه ثم تقايلا البيع ولم يتقابضا حتى اشتراه من البائع جاز شراؤه ولو باعه البائع بعد الإقالة من غير المشتري لا يجوز بيعه .اهـ. وهذا كله في تصرف المشتري في المبيع قبل قبضه
And he (the author) made the ruling general [by saying 'sale'], so it includes the case where he (the buyer) sells it (the item) to his seller before taking possession: it is not valid, and the first sale is not annulled, contrary to the case where he gifts it to him and he accepts it, then it is annulled because gift is a metaphor for iqalah (rescission), unlike sale. And in al-Khaniyya: He bought a slave and took possession, then they rescinded the sale but did not exchange [the item and price] until he bought it from the seller; his purchase is valid. And if the seller, after iqalah, sells it to someone other than the buyer, his sale is not valid. This is all regarding the buyer's disposal of the sold item before taking possession.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the matn, applying the prohibition to specific scenarios. If the buyer sells the item back to the original seller before qabd, the sale is invalid and does not annul the first sale. However, if the buyer gifts it to the seller and the seller accepts, this is treated as iqalah (mutual rescission), annulling the first sale. The example from al-Khaniyya illustrates: after iqalah and before repossession, if the buyer buys it again from the seller, it is valid. But if the seller sells it to a third party after iqalah, that sale is invalid. The passage concludes by noting this is all about the buyer's disposal.

Key Terms
إقالة (iqalah): Mutual rescission of a sale contract.
هبة (hibah): Gift; the transfer of ownership without compensation.
قبض (qabd): Taking physical or constructive possession of the sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The gift is invalid
B) The first sale is rescinded
C) The buyer must still pay the price
D) The seller can sell it to someone else

Key Takeaway
A gift to the seller before qabd, when accepted, operates as an iqalah and rescinds the sale; a sale does not.

Seller's Disposal Before Possession: Buyer's Order and Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Commentary on seller's transactions before qabd with and without buyer's order, including an intra-Hanafi dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on seller's disposal of sold item before possession in two scenarios and state the intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of the buyer's command 'sell it'.

Core Idea
Seller's disposal with buyer's order is valid; without order, buyer has khiyar. The meaning of 'sell it' is disputed within the school.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq tackles a critical issue in sales: what happens if the seller disposes of the item before the buyer takes possession? The answer depends on whether the buyer ordered it. The text also reveals a subtle dispute between the two great Hanafi imams, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, over interpreting the buyer's command 'sell it'. We will walk through the rules and then focus on the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
The passage divides seller's disposal into two: with buyer's order and without. With order, the seller acts as buyer's agent; the disposal is valid and the buyer is considered to have constructive possession. Without order, the buyer has an option (khiyar) to confirm or rescind.
This distinction is foundational.
2
Examine the effect of buyer's order in detail
The text gives examples: gifting, leasing, lending. All are valid with the buyer's order. Also, the rent received by the seller is counted from the price. Note that the order must be for an act the seller can legally do as agent.
The buyer's order effectively transfers agency to the seller.
3
Analyze the three types of command to sell
The passage distinguishes: 'sell it to yourself' (rescission), 'sell it for me' (not a rescission, and the sale is invalid), and general 'sell it' or 'sell it to whomever you wish' (dispute). This leads to the intra-Hanafi dispute.
Focus on the wording: 'bih' (sell it) vs 'bih li' (sell it for me).
4
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Muhammad holds that a general 'sell it' (bih) constitutes a rescission, making the second sale valid. Abu Hanifa holds it is not a rescission, similar to 'sell it for me'. Shaykh al-Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl adds nuance: before possession and sight, it is rescission due to khiyar al-ru'yah; after possession and sight, it is not rescission.
The dispute hinges on the buyer's intention and the effect of khiyar.
5
Review the rules when there is no buyer's order
Without order and without subsequent consent, the seller's disposal (e.g., pawning, leasing, gifting) does not bind the buyer. If the item perishes, the buyer can either confirm the sale and hold the third party liable, or rescind. The text details liability for different acts.
The buyer's khiyar protects against unauthorized actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن تصرف فيه البائع قبل قبضه فهو على وجهين : إما أن يكون بأمر المشتري أو بغير أمره. فإن كان الأول ذكر في الخانية : رجل اشترى عبداً ولم يقبضه فأمره أن يهبه من فلان ففعل البائع ذلك ودفعه إلى الموهوب له جازت الهبة وصار المشتري قابضاً، وكذا لو أمر البائع أن يؤاجره فلاناً معيناً أو غير معين ففعل جاز وصار المستأجر قابضاً للمشتري أولاً ثم يصير قابضاً لنفسه، والأجر الذي يأخذه البائع من المستأجر يحسبه من الثمن إن كانه من جنسه ، وكذا لو أعار العبد البائع (أو بعده بغير إذن البائع الضمير عائد إلى القبض والجار والمجرور متعلق به أو بمحذوف حال منه أي أن القبض الواقع بلا إذن البائع بمنزلة عدم القبض لأن على قول محمد فالظاهر من رجل قبل التسليم إلى المشتري أو وهب أو رهن فأجاز المشتري ذلك جاز اهـ . قال قابضاً ويصير ثم قال : اشترى ثوباً ولم يقبضه ولم ينقد الثمن ثم قال البائع لا أئتمنك عليه ادفعه إلى فلان ففعل كان فسخاً للبيع لأنه لا يصلح نائباً عن المشتري في ذلك فكان مجازاً عن الفسخ ليكون واطئاً وأكلاً مال نفسه وأما الآمر بالبيع فعلى ثلاثة أوجه : إن قال البائع بعه لنفسك فباعه كان فسخاً، وإن قال بعه لي لا يجوز البيع ولا يكون فسخاً، ولو قال بعه أو بعه ممن شئت فباعه كان فسخاً وجاز البيع الثاني للمأمور في قول محمد. وقال أبو حنيفة : لا يكون فسخاً وهو كقوله بعه لي ولو اشترى ثوباً أو حنطة فقال للبائع بعه قال الشيخ الإمام أو بكر محمد بن الفضل : إن كان ذلك قبل قبض المشتري وقبل الرؤية يكون فسخاً، وإن لم يقل البائع نعم لأن المشتري ينفرد بالفسخ في خيار الرؤية. وإن بعه لي أي كن وكيلاً في الفسخ فما لم يقبل البائع ولم يقل نعم لا يكون فسخاً، وإن كان ذلك بعد القبض والرؤية لا يكون فسخاً ويكون وكيلاً بالبيع سواء قال بعه أو بعه لي اهـ. وأما إذا كان بغير أمره ولم يلحقه إجازة فذكر في الخانية : رجل اشترى عبداً بألف ولم يقبضه حتى رهنه البائع أو أجره أو أودعه فمات انفسخ البيع ولا يضمن المشتري أحداً من هؤلاء لأنه إن ضمنهم رجعوا على البائع، ولو أعاره أو وهبه فمات عند المستعير أو الموهوب له أو أودعه فاستعمله المودع . فمات من ذلك كان للمشتري الخيار إن شاء أمضى البيع وضمن المستعير والمودع والموهوب له وإن شاء فسخ البيع لأنه لو ضمن هؤلاء ليس للضامن أن يرجع على البائع ولو باعه البائع فمات عند المشتري الثاني من عمله أو من غير عمله كان المشتري الأول بالخيار إن شاء فسخ البيع وإن شاء ضمن المشتري الثاني ثم يرجع المشتري الثاني على البائع بالثمن إن كان نقده الثمن وإلا لم يرجع ولو أمر البائع رجلاً فقتله كان للمشتري أن يضمن القاتل قيمته لأنه إذا ضمن لم يرجع على البائع، وإن أمر البائع رجلاً بذبح الشاة فذبحها إن كان الذابح بعلم بالبيع فللمشتري تضمينه ولا رجوع له اهـ
If the seller disposes of it (the sold item) before its taking possession, it is in two ways: either by the buyer's order or without his order. If the first, it is mentioned in al-Khaniyya: A man bought a slave and had not taken possession, so he ordered him (the seller) to gift it to so-and-so, and the seller did that and handed it to the donee; the gift is valid and the buyer becomes a possessor. Similarly, if he ordered the seller to lease it to a specified or unspecified person, and he did so, it is valid, and the lessee first becomes a possessor for the buyer then for himself, and the rent that the seller takes from the lessee he counts from the price if it is of its type. Similarly, if the seller lends the slave. (His saying: 'or after it without the seller's permission' — the pronoun refers to qabd, and the prepositional phrase is attached to it or to an omitted hal (circumstantial) meaning that qabd occurring without the seller's permission is like the absence of qabd because the seller has the right to retract it to detain it for the price. His saying: 'and sulh because it is sale' meaning sulh for debt as in al-Fath, and the expression in al-Nahr 'khul'' is a slip of the pen unless from the copyists' distortion. His saying: 'and the principle as in al-Idah' – this principle does not apply according to Muhammad, so it is apparent that... From a man before delivery to the buyer, or he gifted or pawned, and the buyer consented to that, it is valid. Then he said: He bought a garment and did not take possession and did not pay the price, then the seller said: I do not trust you with it; hand it over to so-and-so to keep it until I pay you the price. So the seller gave it to so-and-so, and it perished in his hand; the loss is on the seller because the recipient holds it for the price on behalf of the seller, so his hand is like the seller's hand. And if the buyer ordered the seller to have intercourse with the slave girl or to eat the food, and he did so, it is a rescission of the sale because he is not a valid deputy for the buyer in that, so it is figurative for rescission so that he becomes one who has intercourse and eats his own property. As for the one who commands selling, it is of three types: if the buyer said 'sell it to yourself' and he sold it, it is a rescission; if he said 'sell it for me' the sale is not valid and it is not a rescission; if he said 'sell it' or 'sell it to whomever you wish' and he sold it, it is a rescission and the second sale is valid for the agent according to Muhammad. Abu Hanifa said: it is not a rescission, and it is like saying 'sell it for me'. And if he bought a garment or wheat and said to the seller 'sell it', the Shaykh al-Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: if that is before the buyer's possession and before seeing, it is a rescission, even if the seller did not say 'yes', because the buyer alone has the right of rescission in khiyar al-ru'yah. And if he said 'sell it for me' meaning be a deputy in rescission, as long as the seller has not accepted and said 'yes', it is not a rescission. And if that is after possession and seeing, it is not a rescission, and he is a deputy for sale whether he said 'sell it' or 'sell it for me'. As for when it is without his order and does not receive his consent, it is mentioned in al-Khaniyya: A man bought a slave for one thousand and had not taken possession until the seller pawned it, leased it, or deposited it, and it died; the sale is rescinded, and the buyer does not guarantee any of these persons because if he guarantees them, they would resort to the seller. And if he lent it or gifted it and it died with the borrower, donee, or if he deposited it and the depositary used it and it died thereby, the buyer has the option: if he wishes he confirms the sale and guarantees the borrower, depositary, and donee; if he wishes he rescinds the sale because if he guarantees these, the guarantor cannot resort to the seller. And if the seller sold it and it died with the second buyer from his action or not, the first buyer has the option: if he wishes he rescinds the sale, if he wishes he guarantees the second buyer, then the second buyer may resort to the seller for the price if he had paid it, otherwise not. And if the seller ordered a man to kill it, the buyer may guarantee the killer its value because if he guarantees him he does not resort to the seller. And if the seller ordered a man to butcher a sheep and he butchered it, if the butcher knew of the sale, the buyer may hold him liable and he has no recourse.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the seller's disposal of the sold item before qabd. It covers two scenarios: with and without the buyer's order. With the buyer's order, the seller acts as the buyer's agent; the transaction is valid and the buyer is considered to have constructive possession. Examples include gift, lease, and loan. The text also clarifies the meaning of certain matn phrases, like 'or after it without the seller's permission' indicating that qabd without permission is like no qabd. It then discusses cases from al-Khaniyya: if the buyer orders the seller to do something that only the owner can do (like intercourse or eating), it is treated as rescission. The command to sell is analyzed in three types: if told 'sell to yourself' it's rescission; 'sell for me' it's not; general 'sell' it's rescission per Muhammad, but Abu Hanifa disagrees. Without the buyer's order, the seller's transactions are not binding on the buyer; the buyer has the option (khiyar) to confirm the sale (and hold the third party liable) or rescind. Examples include pawning, leasing, deposit, gift. The passage also covers the death of the item and liability.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or cancellation of a contract.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The option of seeing: the buyer can rescind if the item is not as seen.
قبض (qabd): Taking possession or delivery of the sold item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, if the buyer tells the seller 'sell it' (بعه) without further specification before possession, what is the effect?
A) The sale is rescinded and the seller can sell to a third party
B) The command is invalid
C) The buyer becomes the seller's agent
D) Nothing happens

Key Takeaway
The seller's disposal before possession is valid with the buyer's order; without order, the buyer has khiyar. The meaning of 'sell it' is disputed: Muhammad says rescission, Abu Hanifa says agency, and Ibn al-Fadl reconciles by timing (khiyar al-ru'yah).
