Prohibition of Selling Measured Food Before Re-measuring
Commentary on the prohibition of selling measured food before re-measuring.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a buyer of a measured food item must re-measure it before selling or consuming, and identify the hadith proof.

Core Idea
A measured food item must be re-measured by the buyer before resale to eliminate uncertainty.

This lesson explores a key rule in Hanafi sales law: when you buy a food item sold by measure, you cannot simply trust the seller's measure. You must re-measure it yourself before you sell or consume it. The proof is a hadith requiring two separate measures, and the ruling aims to prevent gharar (uncertainty) in transactions. We will unpack the ruling, its evidence, and an intra-madhhab note.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage states: if a person buys a measured item (mawkilan) by measure (kaylan), it is haram to sell it or consume it until he re-measures it. Note the hukm level: prohibition (haram/tahrimi).
The act is selling or consuming before re-kil.
2
Locate the proof
The author cites a hadith: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) prohibited selling food until two measures have passed: the seller's measure and the buyer's measure.' This is the textual evidence.
The hadith is ahad, so zanni al-thubut.
3
Understand the legal reasoning
The prohibition is based on the necessity of eliminating gharar (uncertainty) about the quantity. The buyer's own re-measurement ensures certainty and prevents disputes.
Gharar is a key principle in Hanafi sales law.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi nuance
The text mentions a possibility that this ruling is specific to Abu Yusuf (i.e., not the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf, but the general mufta bihi position maintains the prohibition.
The OCR is unclear; the exact nature of the specification is uncertain.
5
Apply the ruling
In practice, if you buy wheat by the bushel, you must re-measure it (or have a trusted third-party measure) before you can resell it or even consume it for yourself. This ensures the exact quantity is known.
The rule applies only to items customarily sold by measure (mawkilan).

Source Text

صاع (sa'): A unit of volume measure used for food items.

Invalidity of Selling Before Possession (Qabd)
Commentary on the invalidity of selling an item before the buyer takes possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that a sale of an item before the buyer takes possession is batil (void), and state the consequence of ratification.

Core Idea
Selling an item before the buyer takes possession is batil; it cannot be validated.

This lesson addresses a common scenario in sales: the buyer leaves the purchased item in the seller's custody, and the seller then resells it to a third party. The Hanafi school rules that such a sale is inherently void (batil) because the seller no longer owns the item; the buyer had not taken possession (qabd). Even if the buyer later ratifies, the sale becomes fasid (defective) and must be rescinded. We will unpack this ruling and its practical application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A buyer purchases a house, slaves, or goods (like inventory) but leaves them in the seller's hand. The original seller then sells the same item to a second buyer at a profit.
The key is that the buyer did not take physical possession (qabd).
2
State the ruling on the first sale
The passage says: 'the sale is void' (البَيعُ باطِل). That is, the original seller's subsequent sale to the second buyer is batil (void ab initio) because the seller no longer has ownership or authority to sell.
Batil means the sale has no legal effect.
3
Understand the effect of ratification
If the first buyer ratifies the sale (i.e., approves the original seller's resale), the sale does not become valid; instead, it becomes fasid (defective). The second buyer must return the item, and the contract must be rescinded.
Ratification cannot cure a sale that lacked ownership (ghasb).
4
Note the second buyer's liability
The first buyer (original owner) has the option to hold the second buyer liable for the value of the item on the day the second buyer took possession (qabd). This is a form of compensation.
This is stated earlier in the passage: 'if he wishes, he may hold the second buyer liable for its value.'
5
Apply the principle
In practice, if you buy a car from a dealer but leave it on their lot, the dealer cannot resell it to someone else. If they do, the second sale is void. You can either take the car back from the second buyer or claim its value from them.
The ruling protects the buyer's right to property before possession.

Source Text

His saying: 'And if he buys a measured (item) by measure, its sale and consumption are prohibited until he measures it' i.e., until he re-measures it, because of the prohibition of selling food until two measures pass: the seller's measure and the buyer's measure, and because it is possible that it is specific to Abu Yusuf, reflect. And in it: If he bought a house, slaves, or goods and left them in the seller's hand, and he (the seller) sold them and made a profit, the sale is void; if the buyer ratifies it, it is also fasid because it is a sale of the sold item before possession, and it must be rescinded.

Commentary
This passage comments on a matn ruling about buying measured items (mawkilan). The buyer cannot sell or consume the item until he re-measures it himself. The reason is a hadith that requires two measures: the seller's and the buyer's, to avoid uncertainty. The text notes a potential view that this is specific to Abu Yusuf (but unclear). Then it adds a note from al-Qunyah about the buyer's option to hold the second buyer liable for the value if the seller sold the item before qabd. Finally, it reiterates that if the buyer leaves the item in the seller's hand and the seller sells it at a profit, the sale is batil (void) and if ratified, it becomes fasid and must be rescinded.

Key Terms
قبض (qabd): Taking physical possession of a purchased item.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission of a contract, returning the parties to their pre-contract state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The seller is no longer liable.

Key Takeaway
Selling an item before the buyer takes possession is batil; ratification turns it into a fasid contract that must be rescinded.

Buyer's Commands Before Possession: Dissolution or Agency?
Commentary on buyer's instructions before possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on whether a buyer's command to sell constitutes dissolution of the sale based on the wording used (ʼsell it for yourselfʹ, ʼsell it for meʹ, ʼsell itʹ unqualified).

Core Idea
What a buyer says to the seller changes the legal effect: some commands dissolve the sale, others create an agency.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raʼiq clarifies a critical point in sales before possession: the buyer's verbal instructions to the seller determine if the original sale is dissolved or if an agency is created. The commentary explores three wordings and an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al‑Shaybani on the unqualified command ʼsell itʼ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Setting
The buyer instructs the seller to dispose of the purchased item before the buyer has taken possession. The legal effect depends on the specific wording and the nature of the act commanded.
2
Distinguish Three Forms of the Command to Sell
1) ʼSell it for yourselfʼ (بعه لنفسك) – dissolution. 2) ʼSell it for meʼ (بعه لي) – second sale invalid, no dissolution. 3) ʼSell itʼ (بعه) unqualified – dispute: according to Muhammad it is dissolution; according to Abu Hanifa it is like ʼsell it for meʼ (agency).
The text also mentions ʼsell it to whomever you wishʼ, treated like the unqualified form.
3
Apply the Principle for Personal Acts
If the buyer orders the seller to have intercourse with a slave girl or eat food, this is always a dissolution because the seller cannot act as the buyer's deputy in such personal acts.
The seller is deemed to be acting for himself, thus the original contract is dissolved.
4
Analyse the Intra‑Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds that an unqualified ʼsell itʼ does not dissolve the sale; it makes the seller an agent. Muhammad al‑Shaybani holds it is a dissolution and the second sale is valid for the agent. The commentary presents both without explicit tarjih.
The thamarah al‑khilaf: under Abu Hanifa the original sale remains intact; under Muhammad it is rescinded.
5
Determine the Thamarah al‑Khilaf
If the buyer says just ʼsell itʼ, according to Abu Hanifa the original sale is still valid and the seller becomes an agent – the second sale is valid if the seller sells as agent. According to Muhammad, the original sale is dissolved and the second sale is valid for the agent (the seller).
The practical difference is whether the buyer can still claim the item from the original seller or must deal with the new buyer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب البيع باب المرابحة والتولية من رجل قبل التسليم إلى المشتري أو وهب أو رهن فأجاز المشتري ذلك جاز اهـ . قال قابضاً ويصير ثم قال : اشترى ثوباً ولم يقبضه ولم ينقد الثمن ثم قال البائع لا أئتمنك عليه ادفعه إلى فلان يكون عنده حتى أدفع إليك الثمن فدفعه البائع إلى فلان فهلك من يده كان الهلاك على البائع لأن المدفوع إليه يمسكه للثمن لأجل البائع فتكون يده كيد البائع، ولو أمر المشتري البائع بوطء الجارية أو بأكل الطعام ففعل كان فسخاً للبيع لأنه لا يصلح نائباً عن المشتري في ذلك فكان مجازاً عن الفسخ ليكون واطئاً وأكلاً مال نفسه وأما الآمر بالبيع فعلى ثلاثة أوجه : إن قال البائع بعه لنفسك فباعه كان فسخاً، وإن قال بعه لي لا يجوز البيع ولا يكون فسخاً، ولو قال بعه أو بعه ممن شئت فباعه كان فسخاً وجاز البيع الثاني للمأمور في قول محمد. وقال أبو حنيفة : لا يكون فسخاً وهو كقوله بعه لي ولو اشترى ثوباً أو حنطة فقال للبائع بعه قال الشيخ الإمام أو بكر محمد بن الفضل : إن كان ذلك قبل قبض المشتري وقبل الرؤية يكون فسخاً، وإن لم يقل البائع نعم لأن المشتري ينفرد بالفسخ في خيار الرؤية. وإن بعه لي أي كن وكيلاً في الفسخ فما لم يقبل البائع ولم يقل نعم لا يكون فسخاً، وإن كان ذلك بعد القبض والرؤية لا يكون فسخاً ويكون وكيلاً بالبيع سواء قال بعه أو بعه لي اهـ. وأما إذا كان بغير أمره ولم يلحقه إجازة فذكر في الخانية : رجل اشترى عبداً بألف ولم يقبضه حتى رهنه البائع أو أجره أو أودعه فمات انفسخ البيع ولا يضمن المشتري أحداً من هؤلاء لأنه إن ضمنهم رجعوا على البائع، ولو أعاره أو وهبه فمات عند المستعير أو الموهوب له أو أودعه فاستعمله المودع . فمات من ذلك كان للمشتري الخيار إن شاء أمضى البيع وضمن المستعير والمودع والموهوب له وإن شاء فسخ البيع لأنه لو ضمن هؤلاء ليس للضامن أن يرجع على البائع ولو باعه البائع فمات عند المشتري الثاني من عمله أو من غير عمله كان المشتري الأول بالخيار إن شاء فسخ البيع وإن شاء ضمن المشتري الثاني ثم يرجع المشتري الثاني على البائع بالثمن إن كان نقده الثمن وإلا لم يرجع ولو أمر البائع رجلاً فقتله كان للمشتري أن يضمن القاتل قيمته لأنه إذا ضمن لم يرجع على البائع، وإن أمر البائع رجلاً بذبح الشاة فذبحها إن كان الذابح بعلم بالبيع فللمشتري تضمينه ولا رجوع له اهـ.
The book of sales: chapter of murabahah and tawliyah. From a man before delivery to the buyer, or he gifted or pawned, and the buyer permitted that, it is permissible. End. He said: 'taking possession and it becomes...' Then he said: He bought a cloth and did not take possession and did not pay the price, then the seller said: I do not trust you with it, hand it over to so-and-so to keep with him until I pay you the price, so the seller handed it over to so-and-so and it perished in his hand: the loss is on the seller because the receiver is holding it for the price on behalf of the seller, so his hand is like the seller's hand. And if the buyer ordered the seller to have intercourse with the slave girl or to eat the food and he did so, it is a dissolution of the sale because he is not suitable as a deputy for the buyer in that, so it is interpreted as dissolution so that he becomes the one having intercourse and eating his own property. As for the one ordering to sell, it is of three types: if the seller said 'sell it for yourself' and he sold it, it is a dissolution; if he said 'sell it for me', the sale is not valid and it is not a dissolution; and if he said 'sell it' or 'sell it to whomever you wish' and he sold it, it is a dissolution and the second sale is valid for the agent according to Muhammad. Abu Hanifa said: it is not a dissolution, and it is like his saying 'sell it for me'. And if he bought a cloth or wheat and said to the seller 'sell it' — Shaykh al-Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: if that was before the buyer's possession and before sighting, it is a dissolution, even if the seller did not say 'yes', because the buyer alone has the right to dissolve in the option of sighting. But if he said 'sell it for me', i.e., be an agent in dissolution, then as long as the seller has not accepted and said 'yes', it is not a dissolution. And if that was after possession and sighting, it is not a dissolution and he becomes an agent for sale, whether he said 'sell it' or 'sell it for me'. End. As for when it is without his order and no ratification reaches him, it is mentioned in al-Khaniyyah: a man bought a slave for a thousand and did not take possession until the seller pawned him, or leased him, or deposited him, and he died, the sale is rescinded and the buyer does not guarantee any of these because if he guaranteed them, they would have recourse against the seller. And if he gave him as a gift or loaned him and he died with the borrower or donee or deposited him and the depositee used him and he died from that: the buyer has the choice: if he wishes, he confirms the sale and guarantees the borrower, depositee, and donee; if he wishes, he rescinds the sale because if he guaranteed those, the guarantor has no recourse against the seller. And if the seller sold him and he died with the second buyer from his action or otherwise: the first buyer has the choice: if he wishes, he rescinds the sale; if he wishes, he guarantees the second buyer, then the second buyer has recourse against the seller for the price if he had paid the price, otherwise no. And if the seller ordered a man to kill him and he did so: the buyer may guarantee the killer his value because if he guarantees, he has no recourse against the seller. And if the seller ordered a man to slaughter the sheep and he slaughtered it: if the slaughterer knew of the sale, the buyer may hold him liable and he has no recourse. End.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq discussing various scenarios where the buyer has not yet taken possession of the purchased item. The first ruling states that if the seller gifts or pawns the item before delivery, and the buyer subsequently ratifies the action, it is permissible. The commentary then expands on a case where the seller deposits the cloth with a third party due to distrust; if it perishes, the loss is on the seller because the third party holds it for the seller's benefit. Next, if the buyer orders the seller to do something like having intercourse with a slave girl or eating food, this constitutes a dissolution of the sale because the seller cannot act as the buyer's deputy in such personal actions. Regarding the buyer's command to sell, there are three distinctions: if the seller is told to sell for himself, it is a dissolution; if told to sell for the buyer, the sale is invalid and not a dissolution; if told just 'sell it' without qualification, according to Imam Muhammad it is a dissolution, while Abu Hanifa says it is not, but rather makes the seller an agent for sale. The commentary also addresses the option of sighting before possession. Finally, it discusses cases where the seller pawns, leases, or deposits the item without the buyer's order; the buyer may either confirm the sale and hold the recipient liable or rescind, depending on whether the recipient can recourse against the seller. The key principle is that the seller's unauthorized disposition of the item before delivery gives the buyer a choice, except when the action is such that the recipient cannot recourse against the seller (like gift or loan), in which case the buyer may only rescind or confirm with liability.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Dissolution of the sale contract, returning the parties to their pre-contract positions.
وكيل (wakil): An agent appointed by the buyer to sell the item on his behalf.
إجازة (ijazah): Ratification or permission given by the buyer to validate the seller's previous unauthorized action.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a buyer tells the seller 'sell it for me' (بعه لي), what is the effect on the original sale?
A) The original sale is dissolved.
B) The seller becomes an agent for dissolution.
C) The sale is not valid and it is not a dissolution.
D) The original sale is confirmed and the seller must sell.

Key Takeaway
The wording of the buyer's command to the seller before possession determines whether the sale is dissolved or an agency is created; personal acts always trigger dissolution.

12 min
Prohibition of Selling or Eating Before Remeasuring
Commentary on the core ruling and its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling or eating goods bought by measure before remeasuring, identify the hadith proof, and explain why the act is sinful but the food itself is not haram.

Core Idea
Buying by measure requires remeasuring; selling without it makes the contract fasid and the act haram, but the food remains halal.

In this passage, al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains a key fiqh principle: when you buy a measured commodity (mukeel) by measure, you cannot sell or eat it until you remeasure it. This is based on a prophetic hadith that requires two measures to pass. The commentary clarifies that the prohibition is on the act, not the substance, and distinguishes this from other invalid sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The matn states: if someone buys a measured item by measure, selling it and eating it is forbidden until he remeasures it. The hukm is haram for the act, but the food itself is not haram.
The prohibition applies to disposition before remeasuring.
2
Locate the dalil
The hadith: 'Do not sell food until two measures pass: the seller's measure and the buyer's measure.' This is a zanni al-thubut hadith but yields qati dalala for the prohibition.
The hadith is the primary evidence.
3
Understand the condition 'by measure'
If the item was bought by guess (mujazafah), there is no prohibition because the entire quantity is owned. The condition 'by measure' triggers the obligation to remeasure.
Buying by guess eliminates the need for remeasuring.
4
Grasp the concept of fasid
The sale before remeasuring is invalid (fasid) as per al-Jami' al-Saghir. The prohibition is on the contract itself because the reason for prohibition pertains to the commodity.
Fasid means the contract is not valid but may be rectified; here, it requires remeasuring to become valid.
5
Note the nuance: eating is sinful but not haram food
If the buyer eats without remeasuring, he eats his own property. He sins for neglecting the command but the food is not haram. This distinguishes from invalid sales where the seller has a right to rescind.
The sin is for leaving the obligation of measuring.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولو اشترى مكيلاً كيلاً حرم بيعه وأكله حتى يكيله) أي حتى يعيد كيله لنهيها عن بيع الطعام حتى يجري فيه صاعان صاع البائع وصاع المشتري، ولأنه يحتمل أن أنه خاص بأبي يوسف تأمل. يزيد على المشروط وذلك للبائع والتصرف في مال الغير حرام فيجب التحرز عنه. قيد بقوله «كيلا» أي بشرط الكيل لأنه لو اشتراه مجازفة لا يحرم البيع والأكل قبل الكيل لأن الكل له. ولم يذكر المؤلف فساد البيع ونص في الجامع الصغير على فساده لأن سبب النهي أمر راجع إلى المبيع ولكن النص إنما هو في البيع فألحقوا به منع الأكل قبل الكيل وكل تصرف يبنى على الملك كالهبة والوصية، وألحقوا بالمكيل الموزون. وفي فتح القدير: وينبغي إلحاق المعدود الذي لا يتفاوت كالجوز والبيض إذا اشترى بالعدد وبه قال أبو حنيفة في أظهر الروايتين عنه فأفسد البيع قبل العد اهـ. ولا يلزم من حرمة أكله قبل الإعادة كون الطعام حراماً فقد نص في الجامع الصغير أنه لو أكله وقد قبضه بلا كيل لا يقال إنه أكل حراماً لأنه أكل ملك نفسه إلا أنه يأثم لتركه ما أمر به من الكيل فكان هذا الكلام أصلاً في سائر المبيعات بيعاً فاسداً إذا قبضها فملكها فأكلها، وتقدم أنه لا يحل أكل ما اشتراه فاسداً وهذا يبين أن ليس كل ما لا يحل أكله إذا أكله أن يقال فيه أكل حراماً ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقد ليس يقال هذا كأكل المبيع بيعاً فاسداً لتعلق حق البائع بجميع المبيع ووجوب فسخه، وأما هنا فلا يملك البائع الفسخ ولم يتعلق حق البائع إلا بالزيادة الموهومة فيمكن أن يقال في المبيع فاسداً أكل حراماً، ولكن رأيت في الخلاصة في الأيمان من الثاني عشر في الأكل قال وفي فوائد شمس الأئمة الحلواني : لو أكل من الكرم الذي دفع معاملة وهو قد حلف لا يأكل حراماً لا يحنث، أما عندهما لا يشكل، وعند أبي حنيفة كذلك لأن ذلك عقد فاسد عنده فقد أكل ملك نفسه اهـ . فالحق ما في فتح القدير وإنما ذكر المؤلف كيل المشتري وحده دون كيل البائع مع أن الحديث اشتراط الصاعين لأن صاع البائع ليس بلازم لكل بيع لأن البائع إذا ملكه بالإرث أو المزارعة أو كان شراؤه مجازفة أو استقرض حنطة على أنها كر ثم باعها فالحاجة إلى كيل المشتري وإن كان الاستقراض تمليكاً بعوض كالشراء لكنه شراء صورة عارية حكماً لأن ما المقبوض حكماً ولهذا لم يجب قبض بدله في مال الصرف فكان تمليكاً بلا عوض حكماً. ولو يرده عين قلت : لكن قوله اشترى داراً مبني على قول محمد بفساد بيع العقار قبل قبضه تأمل. قوله : (ولم يذكر المؤلف فساد البيع) أي بيع المشتري لما قاله نوح أفندي أي يحرم عليه بيعه وأكله حتى يكيله أو حتى يعيد الكيل فلو باعه بلا إعادة الكيل يكون البيع فاسداً نص عليه في الجامع الصغير، وقال بقولنا هذا مالك والشافعي وأحمد .اهـ. ومقتضى هذا أن البيع الأول انعقد صحيحاً ولكن حل التصرف فيه من أكل أو بيع موقوف على الكيل وكذا صحة البيع الثاني موقوفة على الكيل ووجه ذلك كما دل عليه الفرع الآتي آخراً عن فتح القدير أن صحة القبض موقوفة على الكيل ولو قبضه بيده لاحتمال الزيادة فإذا باعه قبل كيله فكأنه باع قبل القبض والتصرف في المنقول قبل قبضه لا المسألة من فروع المسألة التي قبلها فإذا أعقبها بها قبل ذكر التصرف في الثمن فتدبر . قوله: (وينبغي إلحاق المعدود الخ) ليس هذا بحثاً فيما لا نقل فيه في المذهب لنا فإنه لقوله وبه قال أبو حنيفة ولأنه كما فكانت هذه
His statement: (And if he buys something measured by measure, selling it and eating it is forbidden until he measures it) i.e., until he remeasures it, because of the prohibition of selling food until two measures pass through it: the seller's measure and the buyer's measure. And because it is probable that it is specific to Abu Yusuf, consider. It exceeds the condition and that is for the seller, and disposing of another's property is forbidden, so one must guard against it. He restricted it by saying 'by measure' i.e., with the condition of measuring, because if he bought it by guess, selling and eating before measuring is not forbidden, because the entire quantity is his. And the author did not mention the invalidity of the sale, but it is stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir that it is invalid, because the reason for the prohibition pertains to the commodity, but the text is only about the sale, so they annexed to it the prohibition of eating before measuring and every disposition based on ownership like gift and bequest. And they annexed to the measured the weighed. And in Fath al-Qadir: It is fitting to annex the counted items that do not differ, like walnuts and eggs, when bought by count, and that is the saying of Abu Hanifa in the more apparent of the two narrations from him, so he rules the sale invalid before counting. End. And it does not follow from the prohibition of eating it before remeasurement that the food is haram; for it is stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir that if he ate it after taking possession without measuring, it is not said that he ate something haram, because he ate his own property, except that he sins for leaving what he was commanded of measuring. So this speech is a principle in all other sales that are invalid: if he takes possession and owns it and eats it, and it was mentioned that it is not permissible to eat what he bought invalidly, and this shows that not everything that it is not permissible to eat, when eaten, is said to be haram; similarly in Fath al-Qadir. And it is not said like eating an item sold in an invalid sale because of the attachment of the seller's right to the entire item and the obligation to rescind. But here the seller does not have the right to rescind, and his right is only attached to the presumed increase, so it is possible to say that in an invalid sale the food is haram. But I saw in al-Khulasa in the section on oaths from the twelfth chapter on eating: It is said in the benefits of Shams al-A'imma al-Hulwani: If he ate from the vineyard that was handed over as a sharecropping contract, and he had sworn not to eat haram, he does not break his oath. As for the two companions, it is not problematic, and according to Abu Hanifa likewise, because that is an invalid contract in his view, so he ate his own property. End. So the truth is what is in Fath al-Qadir. And the author only mentioned the buyer's measure alone, not the seller's measure, despite the hadith requiring two measures, because the seller's measure is not necessary for every sale, because if the seller owned it by inheritance or sharecropping, or his purchase was by guess, or he borrowed wheat on the basis of a kurr and then sold it, the need is for the buyer's measure. Even though borrowing is a transfer of ownership with compensation like purchase, but it is a purchase in form, a loan in substance, because what is received is in substance, and therefore its replacement does not require possession in currency exchange, so it is a transfer of ownership without compensation in substance. And if he rejects it from him, I said: But his statement 'he bought a house' is based on Muhammad's view of invalidity of selling real estate before possession, consider. His statement: (And he did not mention the invalidity of the sale) i.e., the sale of the buyer according to what Nuh Afandi said, i.e., it is forbidden for him to sell it and eat it until he measures it or remeasures it. So if he sells it without remeasuring, the sale is invalid, as stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir. And Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad said our view. End. And this implies that the first sale is valid but the permissibility of disposing of it by eating or selling is suspended on measuring, and likewise the validity of the second sale is suspended on measuring. And the reason for that is as indicated by the following branch later from Fath al-Qadir: that the validity of possession is suspended on measuring. If he took possession with his hand due to the possibility of increase, then if he sold it before measuring, it is as if he sold before possession. And disposing of movable property before possession is not... The issue is from the branches of the issue before it. So if he follows it with it before mentioning the disposal of the price, then consider. His statement: (It is fitting to annex the counted items, etc.) This is not a discussion regarding what there is no transmission in the school for us, because it is according to his statement and Abu Hanifa said it, and because it is as it was.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the ruling that when someone buys an item sold by measure (mukeel), it is forbidden to sell it or eat it until he remeasures it. The commentary explains that this is based on the hadith that food should not be sold until two measures have passed: the seller's and the buyer's. The reason is the possibility of a discrepancy in the measure, which would involve disposing of another's property. The author of the matn restricts the ruling to purchases 'by measure'; if bought by guess (mujazafah), there is no prohibition because the entire quantity is deemed owned by the buyer. The commentary notes that the matn does not explicitly state that the sale before remeasuring is invalid (fasid), but al-Jami' al-Saghir rules it invalid because the prohibition relates to the commodity. This invalidity extends to eating and other dispositions like gift and bequest, and analogously to weighed items. Fath al-Qadir suggests including counted non-varying items like walnuts and eggs; this is Abu Hanifa's view in the more apparent narration. Importantly, the prohibition does not make the food itself haram; if the buyer eats it without remeasuring, he eats his own property but sins by neglecting the command to measure. This is distinguished from eating an item bought in an invalid sale where the seller has a right to rescind. The commentary also explains why the hadith mentions two measures but the matn only mentions the buyer's measure: because the seller's measure is not always needed, e.g., if the seller inherited the goods or bought them by guess. The buyer's measure is necessary to ascertain the exact quantity. The passage concludes that the first sale is valid but disposition is suspended until remeasuring; if sold before, the second sale is invalid. This ruling is agreed upon by Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad.

Key Terms
كيل (kayl): Measurement by volume, typically using a standard measure like a sa' or mudd.
مجازفة (mujazafah): Sale by guess or estimate without precise measurement.
فساد (fasad): Invalidity of a contract due to a defect in its subject matter or condition, but unlike batil, it can be rectified in some cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on the validity of a second sale when a buyer sells goods bought by measure before remeasuring?
A) The second sale is valid.
B) The second sale is invalid (fasid).
C) The second sale is valid only if the buyer has taken possession.
D) The second sale is suspended until the seller agrees.

Key Takeaway
The obligation to remeasure is a necessary condition for disposing of goods bought by measure; failure to do so makes the transaction invalid and sinful, though the goods themselves remain halal.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Extending the Ruling to Counted Items and Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Commentary on extending the ruling to counted items and the intra-Hanafi difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi extension of the remeasuring requirement to counted items like walnuts and eggs, and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on this issue.

Core Idea
According to Abu Hanifa, counted non-varying items require recounting before disposal, analogously to measured goods.

The previous lesson established that goods bought by measure must be remeasured before sale or consumption. But what about items sold by count, such as walnuts or eggs? This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the extension of this ruling to counted items, presenting Abu Hanifa's view and the more apparent narration within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue: extension to counted items
The text asks whether the ruling for measured items should apply to counted non-varying items like walnuts and eggs when bought by count.
The key is that the items do not vary significantly in size or weight.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa extends the ruling: the sale before counting is invalid (fasid), based on the more apparent narration (أظهر الروايتين).
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Present the opposing view (other narration)
The other narration (possibly from Abu Yusuf or Muhammad) does not extend the ruling to counted items, as the hadith specifically mentions kayl (measure).
The text does not name the opposing source but implies a difference within the school.
4
Understand the dalil: qiyas
Abu Hanifa uses analogy (qiyas): the potential for discrepancy exists in both measured and counted items, so the same precaution should apply.
The opposing view restricts the ruling to the literal text.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If someone buys walnuts by count, according to Abu Hanifa they must recount before selling; otherwise the sale is invalid. According to the other view, no recount is needed.
This practical difference affects daily transactions.

Commentary

This invalidity extends to eating and other dispositions like gift and bequest, and analogously to weighed items. Fath al-Qadir suggests including counted non-varying items like walnuts and eggs; this is Abu Hanifa's view in the more apparent narration. Importantly, the prohibition does not make the food itself haram; if the buyer eats it without remeasuring, he eats his own property but sins by neglecting the command to measure. This is distinguished from eating an item bought in an invalid sale where the seller has a right to rescind. The commentary also explains why the hadith mentions two measures but the matn only mentions the buyer's measure: because the seller's measure is not always needed, e.g., if the seller inherited the goods or bought them by guess. The buyer's measure is necessary to ascertain the exact quantity. The passage concludes that the first sale is valid but disposition is suspended until remeasuring; if sold before, the second sale is invalid. This ruling is agreed upon by Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad.

Key Terms
كيل (kayl): Measurement by volume.
عدد (adad): Count; items sold by number.
إلحاق (ilhaq): Annexation; extending a ruling to similar cases via qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the more apparent narration of Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on selling walnuts bought by count before recounting?
A) The sale is valid because walnuts are not measured.
B) The sale is invalid (fasid) because walnuts do not vary significantly.
C) The sale is valid only if the buyer has paid in full.
D) The sale is suspended until the seller agrees.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa extends the remeasuring requirement to counted non-varying items; this is the relied-upon position, though a weaker narration disagrees.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Buyer's Duty to Re-Measure and Invalidity of Premature Sale
Core obligation to re-measure and consequences of non-compliance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a buyer must re-measure goods purchased by measure, explain that selling before re-measuring renders the sale fasid (invalid), and distinguish between the invalidity of the sale and the permissibility of consuming the goods.

Core Idea
Selling before re-measuring is fasid; eating before is a sin but the food is not haram.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the requirement that a buyer must re-measure goods bought on condition of measurement. The author clarifies the legal consequences: selling before re-measuring invalidates the sale, yet the food itself is not unlawful. We'll unpack the reasoning and the evidence from hadith and qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The buyer purchases goods by measure (kayl). He must re-measure after taking possession. If he sells or consumes before re-measuring, the act is prohibited (haram) and the sale is fasid (invalid).
The prohibition applies to all dispositions based on ownership.
2
Locate the evidence
The ruling is based on al-Jami' al-Saghir, which records that the sale before re-measuring is fasid. The hadith 'ashrat al-sa'ayn' (stipulation of two sa's) implies the need for certainty. Qiyas links it to the prohibition of selling food before taking possession.
The hadith is ahad but accepted in Hanafi usul.
3
Distinguish between fasid and prohibition of eating
Although eating before re-measuring is sinful (disobeying a command), the food itself is not haram because the buyer owns it. The seller's right is only to a hypothetical increase, not to rescission. Thus, the buyer's consumption is lawful property.
This is a key Hanafi nuance: not every prohibited act makes the substance haram.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa has two narrations on counted items (e.g., walnuts). The more apparent requires re-counting. Ibn Sam'a reports from the Nawadir that re-measuring is not needed if the seller measured in the buyer's presence. The mufta bihi is the Zahir al-Riwayah requiring re-measurement.
This ikhtilaf affects validity of second sale before measuring.
5
Apply to weighed and counted items
The same rule applies to goods sold by weight or count, except dirhams and dinars. For those, disposition before weighing is permissible because they are fungible and often serve as price.
Length-measured goods (dhira') have different rules – see Lesson 2.

He restricted with his statement 'kaylan' i.e., on condition of measurement because if he bought it without measuring, the sale and consumption before measurement is not prohibited because everything is his. The author did not mention the invalidity of the sale, but in al-Jami' al-Saghir it is stated that it is invalid because the cause of the prohibition is related to the sold item, but the text is only about the sale, so they included the prohibition of consumption before measurement and every disposition based on ownership like gift and bequest, and they included weighed goods with measured. In Fath al-Qadir: one should also include counted items that do not differ like walnuts and eggs if bought by count, and Abu Hanifa said this in the more apparent narration, so he invalidated the sale before counting. The prohibition of eating before remeasurement does not imply that the food is unlawful, as stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir that if he ate it after taking possession without measurement, it is not said that he ate something unlawful because he ate his own property, but he sins for abandoning what he was commanded of measurement. So this statement is a principle for other sales that are invalid: if he takes possession and owns them and eats them, it is not said that he ate something unlawful. In Fath al-Qadir: this is not said like eating something from an invalid sale because the seller's right is attached to the entire sold item and its rescission is obligatory, but here the seller does not have the right to rescission and his right is only attached to the hypothetical increase, so it could be said in the invalid sale that he ate something unlawful. But I saw in al-Khulasa in the chapter of oaths from the twelfth section about eating: in Fawa'id Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani: if he ate from the vine given in mu'amala and he had sworn not to eat anything unlawful, he does not break his oath; according to the two companions there is no problem, and according to Abu Hanifa likewise because that is an invalid contract in his view, so he ate his own property. The correct view is what is in Fath al-Qadir. The author only mentioned the buyer's measurement without the seller's measurement, although the hadith stipulates two sa's, because the seller's sa' is not necessary for every sale. If the seller inherited or received through muzara'a or bought without measurement or borrowed wheat on the basis of a kayl and then sold it, the buyer needs to measure, although borrowing is a transfer of ownership with consideration like purchase, but it is a purchase in form only, because what is received is deemed received in effect, and for this reason its substitute is not required to be taken in exchange in currency transactions, so it is a transfer of ownership without consideration in effect. He said: I say: his statement 'he bought a house' is based on Muhammad's view that the sale of real estate before taking possession is invalid; consider it. His statement: (The author did not mention the invalidity of the sale) i.e., the sale by the buyer according to what Nuh Afandi said: it is prohibited for him to sell and eat until he measures or remeasures; if he sells without remeasuring, the sale is invalid, as stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir, and Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad said the same. The implication is that the first sale was concluded validly, but the permissibility of disposition by sale or consumption is suspended upon measurement, and likewise the validity of the second sale is suspended upon measurement. The evidence, as shown later in Fath al-Qadir, is that the validity of taking possession is suspended upon measurement; if he took possession by hand due to possibility of increase, then if he sells before measuring, it is as if he sold before taking possession, and disposition in movable property before taking possession is not... The issue is a branch of the preceding issue, so he followed it with this before mentioning disposition in the price, so ponder. His statement: (One should also include counted items) This is not a discussion where there is no transmission in our madhhab, because it is according to his statement and Abu Hanifa said it, and because... as it was. The book of sales, chapter of murabaha and tawliya: He must measure it, and likewise weighed and counted except measured by length, and the disposition in the price before taking possession is valid and increase therein. He bought by measure then sold without measurement before measuring and after taking possession; in the apparent narration it is not permissible due to possibility of mixing the seller's property with that of his seller. In the Nawadir of Ibn Sam'a: it is permissible. The seller's measurement is only needed if the seller bought it by measure; the apparent wording of the author indicates that the seller's measurement does not suffice for the buyer's measurement, and this is interpreted as when the seller measured before the sale absolutely or after it in the absence of the buyer. But if he measured in his presence, it suffices for his measurement, and this is correct because the sold item became known by one measurement and the meaning of delivery is realized. The interpretation of the hadith is the conjunction of two transactions as explained in the chapter of salam, God willing. This is from al-Hidaya. From here arises a branch: if food is measured in the presence of a man, then he buys it in the session, then sells it by measure before measuring it after his purchase, this sale is not permissible, whether he measured it for the buyer from him or not, because since he did not measure after his purchase, he is not a possessor, so his sale is sale of what is not in his possession. This is from Fath al-Qadir. His statement: (that is, weighed and counted) meaning like measured: purchase of weighed by weight and counted by count, so sale and consumption are not permissible until he repeats the weighing and counting, and this is restricted to other than dirhams and dinars, for they may be disposed of after taking possession before weighing. This is from al-Idah. It is restricted to the sold item because in al-Muhit: if the measured or weighed item is a price, it is permissible to dispose of it before measurement and weighing because if it is permissible before taking possession, then before measurement is even more so. All this is other than selling by ta'ati; as for that, it is said in al-Qunniya: there is no need for the buyer to weigh again in weighed items because it becomes a sale by taking possession after weighing. And in al-Khulasa: the fatwa is upon this. His statement: (except measured by length) meaning it is prohibited to sell it and dispose of it before repeating the measurement by length after taking possession, even if he bought it on condition of measurement by length, because any increase is for him since length is a description in the cloth, and the possibility of deficiency only gives him an option, and he has waived it by selling, unlike quantity. The apparent meaning of their statement is that if he designated a price for each cubit, it becomes like weighed, and al-Ayni stated it in his commentary on al-Kanz. His statement: (The disposition in the price before taking possession is valid because the absolute ownership stands, and there is no risk of invalidation by destruction due to its non-specificity, unlike the sold item. This is from al-Hidaya, and its apparent meaning is that it is specific to what is not specific, but the ruling is more general, and therefore in Fath al-Qadir: whether it is specific or not, except for the price in currency and salam because what is taken has the status of the sold item, as will come in the text. This is only a preference for the aspect of the chain by the textual proof as in the more apparent narration. His statement: (But if he measured in his presence, it suffices for his measurement) meaning for the buyer's measurement, apparently. Thus the case is: he bought by measure and measured for himself, then sold likewise, and measured in the presence of the buyer from him, that suffices the buyer from his measurement. It is possible that the pronoun refers to the seller, and the case is: he bought by measure and did not measure for himself until he sold to another and measured in the presence of the buyer from him, then it suffices for his measurement i.e., the seller's measurement. This is supported by the justification 'because the sold item became known by one measurement', and this is what is clear from the wording of al-Hidaya, so refer to it, but it is contradicted by his statement 'from here a branch arises'.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage from 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' is an expansion of the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the requirement for a buyer to re-measure purchased goods before any disposition. The author begins by explaining that if a buyer purchases goods on condition of measurement (kayl), he must re-measure them after taking possession. If he sells or consumes them before re-measuring, the sale is fasid (invalid) according to the Zahir al-Riwayah as recorded in al-Jami' al-Saghir. The text clarifies that this is not a prohibition on the food itself being haram; rather, the buyer sins for violating the command to measure. The discussion then extends to weighed and counted items similarly, except dirhams and dinars, which can be disposed of before weighing. There is also an important exception: if the seller measured the goods in the buyer's presence, that measurement suffices, as the goods become known by a single measurement. However, if the goods were measured in the buyer's presence before he purchased them, he still must measure after purchase because he did not measure as owner. Finally, the passage addresses the price (thaman): it is permissible to dispose of it before receiving it, unlike the sold item, because ownership is absolute and there is no risk of the contract invalidating. This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that the price is not subject to the same uncertainty as the sold item.

Key Terms
كيل (kayl): Measurement of volume, e.g., for grains. The condition of purchasing by measure.
مكايلة (mukayala): Sale by measure, i.e., with stipulation of measurement.
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): An invalid sale that is not void but requires rescission; the sold item is owned but subject to return.
ثمن (thaman): The price in a sale; the author states it can be disposed of before receipt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, what is the ruling if a buyer purchases grain by measure and then sells it before re-measuring?
A) The sale is valid and permissible.
B) The sale is fasid (invalid) and the buyer sins.
C) The sale is batil (void) from the start.
D) The sale is valid only if the seller measured in his presence.

Key Takeaway
A buyer must re-measure goods purchased by measure; premature sale is fasid, and while eating is sinful, the food is not haram.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Disposition of Price and Special Cases in Measurement
Ruling on using price before receipt and exceptions (bai' al-ta'ati, seller's measurement in presence, length-measured goods)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling that the price (thaman) may be disposed of before receipt, identify exceptions for dirhams/dinars, and explain when the seller's measurement suffices for the buyer and when it does not.

Core Idea
Price can be used before receipt; seller's measurement in buyer's presence suffices; no re-weighing in bai' al-ta'ati.

This part of the passage addresses an important distinction: while the sold item requires re-measurement, the price can be spent immediately. Additionally, special scenarios like customary exchange (bai' al-ta'ati) and the effect of the seller measuring in the buyer's presence are clarified. Let's examine these exceptions and their rationale.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling on price disposition
The price (thaman) can be disposed of (used, gifted, sold) before the buyer actually receives it or measures it. This is because ownership of the price is absolute and there is no risk of the contract invalidating (no gharar).
Exception for price in currency exchange (sarf) and salam – those have specific rules.
2
Distinguish from the sold item
The sold item is subject to re-measurement because its quantity must be certain; the price is not, because it is usually specific (e.g., currency). The reason: the price is not at risk of increase/decrease via measurement; it is known by specification.
If the price itself is a commodity (e.g., wheat as payment for another item), then it must be measured if bought by measure.
3
Consider weighed and counted items as price
If dirhams and dinars (or other fungible currency) are the price, they can be used before weighing. But if the sold item is measured/weighed/counted, the rule for the sold item applies. The passage notes: 'if the measured or weighed item is a price, it is permissible to dispose of it before measurement/weighing'.
This is from al-Muhit: because if it's permissible before possession, even more so before measurement.
4
Examine bai' al-ta'ati
In customary exchange (bai' al-ta'ati) – buying without explicit contract – once the buyer weighs the goods at the time of taking, no second weighing is needed. The fatwa is upon this view from al-Khulasa.
This is a practical dispensation for retail transactions.
5
Analyze the effect of the seller's measurement
If the seller measured the goods in the buyer's presence after the sale, that measurement suffices for the buyer; he does not need to re-measure. However, if the seller measured before the sale or in the buyer's absence, the buyer must still measure. Also, if goods were measured in the presence of a man who then buys them in the same session, he must re-measure after purchase because his ownership is new.
This is from al-Hidaya; the 'branch' mentioned illustrates the subtlety.

Source Text

بيعه والتصرف فيه قبل إعادة الذرع بعد القبض وإن كان اشتراه بشرط الذرع لأن الزيادة له إذ الذرع وصف في الثوب واحتمال النقص إنما يوجب خياره وقد أسقطه ببيعه بخلاف القدر، وظاهر كلامهم أنه لو أفرد لكل ذراع ثمناً صار كالموزون وقد صرح به العيني في شرح الكنز. قوله : (وصح التصرف في الثمن قبل قبضه لقيام المطلق وهو الملك وليس فيه غرر الانفساخ بالهلاك لعدم تعينها بالتعيين بخلاف المبيع؛ كذا في الهداية وظاهره أنه مخصوص بما لا يتعين والحكم أعم منه ولذا قال في فتح القدير : سواء كان مما يتعين أو لا سوى بدل الصرف والسلم لأن للمقبوض حكم عين المبيع سيأتي متناً وإنما هو استظهار لوجه الحلقة بالمنصوص عليه في الحديث كما هو أظهر الروايتين. قوله : (أما إذا كان في حضرته فإنه يغني عن كيله أي عن كيل المشتري قيماً يظهر، وعليه فصورة المسألة اشتراه مكايلة وكاله لنفسه ثم باعه كذلك وكاله بحضرة المشتري منه أغنى ذلك المشتري عن كيله . ويحتمل عود الضمير إلى البائع وصورته اشتراه مكايلة ولم يكله لنفسه حتى باعه من آخر وكاله بحضرة المشتري منه فإنه يغني عن كيله أي كيل البائع، ويدل على هذا التعليل بقوله لأن المبيع صار معلوماً بكيل واحد وهذا هو المتبادر من كلام الهداية فراجعه لكن ينافيه قوله ومن هنا ينشأ فرع
He restricted with his statement 'kaylan' i.e., on condition of measurement because if he bought it without measuring, the sale and consumption before measurement is not prohibited because everything is his. The author did not mention the invalidity of the sale, but in al-Jami' al-Saghir it is stated that it is invalid because the cause of the prohibition is related to the sold item, but the text is only about the sale, so they included the prohibition of consumption before measurement and every disposition based on ownership like gift and bequest, and they included weighed goods with measured. In Fath al-Qadir: one should also include counted items that do not differ like walnuts and eggs if bought by count, and Abu Hanifa said this in the more apparent narration, so he invalidated the sale before counting. The prohibition of eating before remeasurement does not imply that the food is unlawful, as stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir that if he ate it after taking possession without measurement, it is not said that he ate something unlawful because he ate his own property, but he sins for abandoning what he was commanded of measurement. So this statement is a principle for other sales that are invalid: if he takes possession and owns them and eats them, it is not said that he ate something unlawful. In Fath al-Qadir: this is not said like eating something from an invalid sale because the seller's right is attached to the entire sold item and its rescission is obligatory, but here the seller does not have the right to rescission and his right is only attached to the hypothetical increase, so it could be said in the invalid sale that he ate something unlawful. But I saw in al-Khulasa in the chapter of oaths from the twelfth section about eating: in Fawa'id Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani: if he ate from the vine given in mu'amala and he had sworn not to eat anything unlawful, he does not break his oath; according to the two companions there is no problem, and according to Abu Hanifa likewise because that is an invalid contract in his view, so he ate his own property. The correct view is what is in Fath al-Qadir. The author only mentioned the buyer's measurement without the seller's measurement, although the hadith stipulates two sa's, because the seller's sa' is not necessary for every sale. If the seller inherited or received through muzara'a or bought without measurement or borrowed wheat on the basis of a kayl and then sold it, the buyer needs to measure, although borrowing is a transfer of ownership with consideration like purchase, but it is a purchase in form only, because what is received is deemed received in effect, and for this reason its substitute is not required to be taken in exchange in currency transactions, so it is a transfer of ownership without consideration in effect. He said: I say: his statement 'he bought a house' is based on Muhammad's view that the sale of real estate before taking possession is invalid; consider it. His statement: (The author did not mention the invalidity of the sale) i.e., the sale by the buyer according to what Nuh Afandi said: it is prohibited for him to sell and eat until he measures or remeasures; if he sells without remeasuring, the sale is invalid, as stated in al-Jami' al-Saghir, and Malik, al-Shafi'i, and Ahmad said the same. The implication is that the first sale was concluded validly, but the permissibility of disposition by sale or consumption is suspended upon measurement, and likewise the validity of the second sale is suspended upon measurement. The evidence, as shown later in Fath al-Qadir, is that the validity of taking possession is suspended upon measurement; if he took possession by hand due to possibility of increase, then if he sells before measuring, it is as if he sold before taking possession, and disposition in movable property before taking possession is not... The issue is a branch of the preceding issue, so he followed it with this before mentioning disposition in the price, so ponder. His statement: (One should also include counted items) This is not a discussion where there is no transmission in our madhhab, because it is according to his statement and Abu Hanifa said it, and because... as it was. The book of sales, chapter of murabaha and tawliya: He must measure it, and likewise weighed and counted except measured by length, and the disposition in the price before taking possession is valid and increase therein. He bought by measure then sold without measurement before measuring and after taking possession; in the apparent narration it is not permissible due to possibility of mixing the seller's property with that of his seller. In the Nawadir of Ibn Sam'a: it is permissible. The seller's measurement is only needed if the seller bought it by measure; the apparent wording of the author indicates that the seller's measurement does not suffice for the buyer's measurement, and this is interpreted as when the seller measured before the sale absolutely or after it in the absence of the buyer. But if he measured in his presence, it suffices for his measurement, and this is correct because the sold item became known by one measurement and the meaning of delivery is realized. The interpretation of the hadith is the conjunction of two transactions as explained in the chapter of salam, God willing. This is from al-Hidaya. From here arises a branch: if food is measured in the presence of a man, then he buys it in the session, then sells it by measure before measuring it after his purchase, this sale is not permissible, whether he measured it for the buyer from him or not, because since he did not measure after his purchase, he is not a possessor, so his sale is sale of what is not in his possession. This is from Fath al-Qadir. His statement: (that is, weighed and counted) meaning like measured: purchase of weighed by weight and counted by count, so sale and consumption are not permissible until he repeats the weighing and counting, and this is restricted to other than dirhams and dinars, for they may be disposed of after taking possession before weighing. This is from al-Idah. It is restricted to the sold item because in al-Muhit: if the measured or weighed item is a price, it is permissible to dispose of it before measurement and weighing because if it is permissible before taking possession, then before measurement is even more so. All this is other than selling by ta'ati; as for that, it is said in al-Qunniya: there is no need for the buyer to weigh again in weighed items because it becomes a sale by taking possession after weighing. And in al-Khulasa: the fatwa is upon this. His statement: (except measured by length) meaning it is prohibited to sell it and dispose of it before repeating the measurement by length after taking possession, even if he bought it on condition of measurement by length, because any increase is for him since length is a description in the cloth, and the possibility of deficiency only gives him an option, and he has waived it by selling, unlike quantity. The apparent meaning of their statement is that if he designated a price for each cubit, it becomes like weighed, and al-Ayni stated it in his commentary on al-Kanz. His statement: (The disposition in the price before taking possession is valid because the absolute ownership stands, and there is no risk of invalidation by destruction due to its non-specificity, unlike the sold item. This is from al-Hidaya, and its apparent meaning is that it is specific to what is not specific, but the ruling is more general, and therefore in Fath al-Qadir: whether it is specific or not, except for the price in currency and salam because what is taken has the status of the sold item, as will come in the text. This is only a preference for the aspect of the chain by the textual proof as in the more apparent narration. His statement: (But if he measured in his presence, it suffices for his measurement) meaning for the buyer's measurement, apparently. Thus the case is: he bought by measure and measured for himself, then sold likewise, and measured in the presence of the buyer from him, that suffices the buyer from his measurement. It is possible that the pronoun refers to the seller, and the case is: he bought by measure and did not measure for himself until he sold to another and measured in the presence of the buyer from him, then it suffices for his measurement i.e., the seller's measurement. This is supported by the justification 'because the sold item became known by one measurement', and this is what is clear from the wording of al-Hidaya, so refer to it, but it is contradicted by his statement 'from here a branch arises'.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage from 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' is an expansion of the ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding the requirement for a buyer to re-measure purchased goods before any disposition. The author begins by explaining that if a buyer purchases goods on condition of measurement (kayl), he must re-measure them after taking possession. If he sells or consumes them before re-measuring, the sale is fasid (invalid) according to the Zahir al-Riwayah as recorded in al-Jami' al-Saghir. The text clarifies that this is not a prohibition on the food itself being haram; rather, the buyer sins for violating the command to measure. The discussion then extends to weighed and counted items similarly, except dirhams and dinars, which can be disposed of before weighing. There is also an important exception: if the seller measured the goods in the buyer's presence, that measurement suffices, as the goods become known by a single measurement. However, if the goods were measured in the buyer's presence before he purchased them, he still must measure after purchase because he did not measure as owner. Finally, the passage addresses the price (thaman): it is permissible to dispose of it before receiving it, unlike the sold item, because ownership is absolute and there is no risk of the contract invalidating. This is consistent with the Hanafi principle that the price is not subject to the same uncertainty as the sold item.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): The price in a sale; the author states it can be disposed of before receipt.
بيع تعاط (bay' ta'ati): A sale by mutual exchange without formal offer and acceptance, e.g., buying goods and paying in a shop.
مذروع (madhr'u): Goods sold by length (dhira'), e.g., cloth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does the seller's measurement of goods suffice for the buyer, allowing the buyer to resell without re-measuring?
A) Always, because the seller is trustworthy.
B) If the seller measured after the sale in the buyer's presence.
C) If the seller measured before the sale in the buyer's presence.
D) Never, the buyer must always re-measure.

Key Takeaway
The price can be spent before receipt; seller's measurement in buyer's presence after sale suffices; length-measured goods have different rules.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Ruling on Re-Measurement for Weighed, Counted, and Length-Measured Goods
Ruling extension and exceptions for mawzūn, ma'dūd, and madhrū'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on re-weighing/re-counting for weighed, counted, and length-measured goods, and identify the exceptions for dirhams, dinars, and bai' al-ta'āṭī.

Core Idea
Weighed and counted goods require re-measurement after possession, except for dirhams/dinars and bai' al-ta'āṭī; length-measured goods similarly require it, but separate pricing per unit changes the ruling.

This passage extends the ruling on measured (makīl) goods to weighed (mawzūn) and counted (ma'dūd) items, with important exceptions. Understanding when re-weighing is required is essential for valid sales in Hanafi fiqh. The text also clarifies the special case of length-measured (madhrū') goods, where each cubit priced separately transforms the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general requirement
After possession of mithli goods (makīl, mawzūn, ma'dūd), one must re-measure before disposal. Sale or consumption before re-measurement is not permissible.
2
Recognize the exceptions
Dirhams and dinars (thaman) are excepted; disposal before weighing is permissible. Also, bai' al-ta'āṭī (sale by mutual delivery) does not require re-weighing; the fatwa is upon this.
3
Apply to length-measured goods
Madhrū' goods require re-measuring length after possession. However, if each unit is priced separately, they become like weighed goods and re-measurement is required.
The underlying reason: length is a description (waṣf), deficiency gives a khiyar (option); quantity (qadr) uncertainty makes the sale fasid.
4
Understand the consequence of non-compliance
Selling before re-measurement is makruh tahrimi and renders the sale fasid (invalid) due to uncertainty in quantity, except where khiyar applies for madhrū'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومثله الموزون والمعدود أي مثل المكيل شراء الموزون وزناً والمعدود عدداً فلا يجوز البيع والأكل حتى يعيد الوزن والعد وهو مقيد بغير الدراهم والدنانير أما هما فيجوز التصرف فيهما بعد القبض قبل الوزن؛ كذا في الإيضاح وقيد بالمبيع لما في المحيط : لو كان المكيل أو الموزون ثمناً يجوز التصرف فيه قبل الكيل والوزن لأنه إذا جاز قبل القبض فقبل الكيل أولى. وهذا كله في غير بيع التعاطي، أما هو فقال في القنية : ولا يحتاج في بيع التعاطي في الموزونات إلى وزن المشتري ثانياً لأنه صار بيعاً بالقبض بعد الوزن اهـ. وفي الخلاصة : وعليه الفتوى . قوله : (لا المذروع) أي يحرم بيعه والتصرف فيه قبل إعادة الذرع بعد القبض وإن كان اشتراه بشرط الذرع لأن الزيادة له إذ الذرع وصف في الثوب واحتمال النقص إنما يوجب خياره وقد أسقطه ببيعه بخلاف القدر، وظاهر كلامهم أنه لو أفرد لكل ذراع ثمناً صار كالموزون وقد صرح به العيني في شرح الكنز
His saying: (And similarly the weighed and the counted) i.e., like the measured by volume, buying the weighed by weight and the counted by number: so sale and consumption are not permissible until he re-weighs and re-counts. This is restricted to other than dirhams and dinars; as for them, disposal is permissible after possession before weighing; this is from al-Īḍāḥ. And it is restricted to the sold item because in al-Muḥīṭ: if the measured or weighed is a price, disposal is permissible before measurement and weighing because if it is permissible before possession, then before measurement is more so. All this applies to other than bai' al-ta'āṭī; as for it, it is said in al-Quniyah: there is no need in bai' al-ta'āṭī for weighed items for the buyer to weigh a second time because it becomes a sale by possession after weighing. In al-Khulāṣah: and upon this the fatwa. His saying: (not the measured by length) i.e., it is prohibited to sell it and dispose of it before repeating the measurement by length after possession, even if he bought it with the condition of length because the increase is for him since length is a description in the cloth, and the possibility of deficiency only gives him an option which he has waived by selling it, unlike quantity. The apparent meaning of their speech is that if he assigned a price for each cubit, it becomes like the weighed, and al-'Aynī explicitly stated this in Sharḥ al-Kanz.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage extends the previous ruling on measured (makīl) goods to weighed (mawzūn) and counted (ma'dūd) items. The commentator explains that the same prohibition applies: one cannot sell or consume such goods without re-weighing or re-counting after possession. However, this does not apply to dirhams and dinars (gold and silver coins) because they are considered prices (thaman) and are exceptions; disposal is allowed before weighing. Additionally, if the measured or weighed item is itself a price (as in when money is measured or weighed), disposal is permissible even before measurement or weighing, analogously to before possession. The rule is also relaxed in bai' al-ta'āṭī (sale by mutual delivery without verbal offer/acceptance) where the buyer need not re-weigh because the weight is confirmed at the time of taking possession. The fatwa upholds this. Then the text covers 'madhrū'' (items sold by length, like cloth): the prohibition applies to selling or disposing of it before re-measuring the length after possession. The reasoning is that length is a description (waṣf) of the cloth; any increase belongs to the buyer, but any deficiency gives him an option (khiyār) that he has waived by selling. This is different from quantity (qadr) where the sale would be invalid due to ignorance. However, if each cubit is assigned a separate price, it becomes like weighed items and must be re-measured accordingly.

Key Terms
موزون (mawzūn): Items sold by weight (e.g., wheat, rice).
معدود (ma'dūd): Items sold by count (e.g., eggs, fruits).
مذروع (madhrū'): Items sold by length (e.g., cloth).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT required to be re-weighed or re-measured after possession according to the Hanafi school?
A) Wheat bought by weight
B) Cloth bought by length
C) Gold coins (dinars) bought by weight
D) Eggs bought by count

Key Takeaway
Weighed, counted, and length-measured goods must be re-measured after possession before disposal, except for dirhams and dinars (thaman) and bai' al-ta'āṭī; for length-measured goods, separate pricing per unit makes them like weighed goods, still requiring re-measurement.
